The First Obligation: Knowledge through Proof, Not Imitation
Sanusi states the first duty of the responsible person and warns against blind imitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the first duty of a person who has reached legal responsibility (mukallaf) is to seek certain knowledge of God through rational proofs, and that blind imitation (taqlid) is insufficient for true faith.

Core Idea
The first duty is to know God through decisive proofs, not through imitation.

This passage opens Sanusi's theological treatise with a fundamental principle: before any act of worship, a person must have certain knowledge of God. Sanusi makes clear that merely following what one's family believes (taqlid) is not enough for true faith. He then issues a warning: those who rely on imitation risk doubt when faced with life's trials. The passage sets the stage for the entire work by establishing that rational inquiry is an individual obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Let the author's words and tone set the foundation.
This passage is the opening of Sanusi's work, so it sets the stage.

Notice that Sanusi first states the obligation to use one's intellect to reach knowledge of God through decisive proofs. Then he warns against being satisfied with taqlid for one's creedal beliefs.
3
Reflect on the warning against taqlid
Sanusi gives two reasons: taqlid is not salvific in the afterlife, and it leaves one vulnerable to doubt during trials like the grave. Think about why this matters for a believer.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the complete argument requires the next page.

Commentary
This passage is the opening of Sanusi's work, containing the author's introduction followed by the statement of the first obligation upon the legally responsible person (mukallaf) and a warning against blind imitation (taqlid). After the basmalah and hamdalah, Sanusi prays for guidance and then declares that the very first duty of a person who has reached maturity is to apply his intellect to reach certain knowledge of God through decisive proofs (barahin qati'ah) and brilliant evidences (adillah sati'ah). This is the primary obligation; only if he already has such knowledge before puberty may he turn to other matters in order of priority. Then he explicitly warns against being satisfied with taqlid for one's creedal beliefs, because according to many scholars of verification (muhaqqiqun), taqlid does not save one in the afterlife. Moreover, the person who merely imitates is vulnerable to doubt when confronted with doubts and severe trials such as the grave and the like, which require firm conviction. The passage is cut off mid-sentence at the end of the page.

The First Obligation: Using Intellect to Know Allah
Sanusi states the first obligation upon the mukallaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the very first obligation upon a person who reaches puberty is to use their intellect to attain knowledge of Allah through decisive proofs.

Core Idea
Knowledge of Allah must be acquired through rational proofs, not blind acceptance.

Sanusi opens his treatise with a clear directive: before any act of worship, before anything else, the person who has reached the age of responsibility must engage their intellect to know their Creator. This passage establishes the priority of theological knowledge based on evidence. It is the foundation of all subsequent obligations.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening invocation
Notice the basmalah and praise of Allah, then read Sanusi's address to the reader. Understand that this is a teaching context.
2
Identify the first obligation
Find the sentence stating the first thing obligatory upon one who has reached puberty. Notice it is to 'use his intellect to attain knowledge of his Worshiped through decisive proofs.'
This is the core of the lesson.
3
Understand the exception
Sanusi allows that if one already has this knowledge before puberty, they may move on to other matters. Consider what that implies about the priority of knowledge.
4
Reflect on the phrase 'decisive proofs'
The proofs are described as 'decisive' (qati'a) and 'brilliant' (sati'a). Ask yourself why certainty requires such rigorous evidence.
This connects to later sections on taqlid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم و به نستعین الحمدُ للهِ ربِّ العالمينَ ، والصلاة والسلام على سيدنا ومولانا محمَّدٍ خاتم النبيِّينَ وإمام المرسلينَ ، ورضي الله أصحاب رسولِ اللهِ أجمعين ، وعن التابعينَ ومَنْ تبعَهم بإحسان إلى يوم الدين . [ أوَّلُ واجب على المكلَّف ] اعلم - شرحَ اللهُ صدري وصدرَكَ ، ويَسَّرَ لنيل الكمال في الدارين أمري وأمرَكَ - : أنَّ أَوَّلَ ما يجبُ قبلَ كُلِّ شيءٍ على مَنْ بَلَغَ : أنْ يُعمِلَ فكرَهُ فيما يُوصِلُهُ إلى العلم بمعبودِهِ مِنَ البراهين القاطعة والأدلَّةِ الساطعة ، إلا أنْ يكونَ حصل له العلم بذلك قبل البلوغ ، فليشتغل بعده بالأهم فالأهم [ الكلام على التقليد ] ولا يرضى لعقائدِهِ حرفة التقليد ؛ فإِنَّهَا غيرُ مُخلّصةٍ في الآخرةِ عند كثير مِنَ المحققين . ويُخشى على صاحبها الشكُ عند عروض الشبهات ونزول الدواهي المعضلاتِ ؛ كالقبر ونحوه ، مما يفتقر إلى قول ثابت بالأدلة وقُوَّةِ يقين وعَقْدِ راسخ لا يتزلزل ؛ لكونه نتج عن قواطع البراهين . ولا يغتر المقلد ويستدلُّ أنَّهُ على الحقِّ بِقُوَّةِ تصميمِهِ وكثرة تعبدِهِ ؛ للنقض عليهِ بتصميم اليهود والنصارى وعبدة الأوثانِ ومَنْ في معناهم ؛ تقليداً لأحبارهم وآبائهم الضالين المضلين
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. And with Him we seek help. Praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds, and prayers and peace be upon our master and patron Muhammad, the seal of the prophets and the imam of the messengers, and may Allah be pleased with all the companions of the Messenger of Allah, and with the followers and those who followed them in excellence until the Day of Judgment. [The First Obligation upon the Legally Responsible Person] Know - may Allah expand my chest and your chest, and make easy for us to attain perfection in both abodes my affair and your affair - that the first thing obligatory before everything upon one who has reached puberty is that he use his intellect to attain knowledge of his Worshiped through decisive proofs and brilliant evidences, unless he has obtained knowledge of that before puberty; then he should occupy himself after that with the most important then the next most important. [Discussion on Taqlid] And he should not be content with the craft of taqlid for his beliefs; for verily it is not salvific in the Hereafter according to many of the verifiers. And it is feared for its possessor doubt at the occurrence of ambiguities and the descent of overwhelming calamities; like the grave and the like, which require a firm statement based on evidences and strength of certainty and a solid contract that does not waver; because it results from decisive proofs. And the one who practices taqlid should not be deluded and use as evidence that he is upon truth by the strength of his determination and the abundance of his worship; because this is refuted by the determination of the Jews, Christians, idolaters, and those like them; due to taqlid of their rabbis and their fathers who erred and led others astray.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is an introduction that both praises Allah and sends blessings on the Prophet, then states the first obligation upon the morally responsible person (mukallaf). The author, Sanusi, explains that the very first thing required of someone who has reached puberty (balagh) is to use his intellect (fikr) to arrive at knowledge (ilm) of his deity (ma'bud) through decisive proofs (barahin qati'a) and brilliant evidences (adilla sati'a). There is an exception: if one already has that knowledge before puberty, he may turn to other matters of increasing importance. Then Sanusi discusses taqlid (blind imitation). He warns that one should not be satisfied with taqlid for his beliefs, because according to many verifying scholars (muhaqqiqun), taqlid is not salvific in the Hereafter. Furthermore, the one who relies on taqlid risks falling into doubt when faced with ambiguities (shubuhat) and severe trials like the grave, which require firm conviction based on proofs. He then refutes a possible excuse: a muqallid might think that his strong determination and abundant worship prove he is on the truth, but Sanusi points out that Jews, Christians, and idolaters also have strong determination and worship due to taqlid of their leaders, so that argument is invalid.

Key Terms
واجب
مكلف
براهين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the first obligation is a specific act of worship like prayer or fasting.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly states that the first obligation is to acquire knowledge of Allah through proofs, before any other duty.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the first thing obligatory upon a person who has reached puberty?
A) Performing the five daily prayers
B) Using his intellect to attain knowledge of Allah through decisive proofs
C) Following the scholars in taqlid
D) Giving charity

Key Takeaway
The very first duty of every accountable person is to use their reason to know Allah through certain proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever truly examined the proofs for your belief in Allah, or have you inherited your faith without personal conviction? What would it cost you to sincerely seek decisive evidence today?
?
If the first obligation upon you is to use your intellect to know Allah, why do you spend more time on other religious duties while neglecting this foundational one? Repent and resolve to study the rational proofs.
?
Imagine standing before Allah and He asks you: 'Did you use the reason I gave you to know Me with certainty?' What will you answer? Start now to build that certainty through the proofs Sanusi mentions.

Warning Against Blind Imitation (Taqlid) in Beliefs
Sanusi warns against taqlid and refutes its validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why taqlid is insufficient for saving belief, and how it exposes one to doubt and is refuted by the example of other religions.

Core Idea
Taqlid is not salvific and leads to doubt; only certainty from proofs withstands trials.

After establishing the first obligation to seek knowledge through proofs, Sanusi now warns against the dangerous shortcut of taqlid—blind imitation of others in matters of belief. He explains why taqlid is not acceptable in the Hereafter, and he refutes the false comfort that strong determination or abundant worship can substitute for evidence-based certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on taqlid
Read from 'ولا يرضى لعقائده حرفة التقليد' to the end. Note the strong language: taqlid is called a 'craft' and is said to be non-salvific.
2
Identify the harm of taqlid
Sanusi says taqlid brings fear of doubt during trials like the grave. Understand that without proofs, belief can waver.
3
Analyze the refutation of false confidence
Sanusi warns that a muqallid should not be deluded by his own determination and worship, because Jews, Christians, and idolaters also have strong determination due to taqlid.
This is a powerful argument against relying on sincerity alone.
4
Reflect on the role of proofs
The passage emphasizes that true faith requires 'firm statement based on evidences' and 'decisive proofs.' Consider how this relates to the first obligation.
This ties back to lesson 1.

Scriptural Proof for Tawhid
Sanusi discusses the validity of using scriptural proof for tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether scriptural proof can be used to establish the oneness of God.

Core Idea
Scriptural proof for tawhid is valid but some scholars object.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a nuanced position on the use of scriptural evidence for proving divine unity. He acknowledges its validity but also reports the objection of some verifiers. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind the objection and clarifies the interplay between rational and scriptural proofs in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the core argument.
Pay attention to the key terms: العقد, الوحدانية, الدليل السمعي.
2
Identify the two positions
Notice that Sanusi first states it is valid to use scriptural proof, then mentions that some verifiers rejected it. Identify the 'why' behind the rejection.
The objection is based on the dependency of tawhid on the existence of the Creator.
3
Analyze the reasoning of the objectors
Understand the logical chain: scriptural proof cannot establish the existence of the Creator (since scripture presupposes a Creator), therefore it cannot establish what depends on that existence (i.e., tawhid).
This argument underscores the need for rational proof for God's existence before scripture.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this debate matters: it highlights the relationship between reason and revelation in Islamic theology. Tawhid can be known through both, but the priority of rational proof is affirmed by some.
Sanusi does not endorse either view definitively, leaving it as an open opinion.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to verify you have grasped the key point of the objection.
The answer is based directly on the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويصح إثباتُ هذا العَقْدِ ـ وهو الوحدانية ـ بالدليل السمعي ، ومنعَهُ بعضُ المحققين ، وهو رأي ؛ لأنَّ ثبوت الصانع لا يتحقَّقُ بدونِها ، ولا أثر للدليل السمعي في ثبوتِ الصانع ، فكذا ما يتوقَّفُ عليه ، والله أعلم .
And it is valid to establish this tenet—that is, Unity (waḥdāniyya)—by scriptural proof (al-dalīl al-samʿī). But some verifiers (muḥaqqiqūn) prevented that, and it is an opinion, because the establishment of the Maker (al-Ṣāniʿ) is not realized without it (i.e., without unity), and scriptural proof has no effect in establishing the Maker. So likewise what depends on it. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage addresses the use of scriptural evidence (samʿī dalīl) to prove the oneness of God. The author acknowledges that it is valid to use scriptural proof for tawhid, but notes that some scholars (called 'muḥaqqiqūn' or verifiers) objected to this. Their reasoning is that the existence of the Creator (al-Ṣāniʿ) cannot be established by scripture alone—since scripture itself presupposes a creator—and unity is a necessary condition for the existence of a creator. Therefore, if scriptural proof cannot establish the existence of the Creator, it cannot establish what depends on that existence, namely unity. The author does not necessarily endorse this view but presents it as an opinion.

Key Terms
العقد
الوحدانية
الدليل السمعي
الثبوت
الصانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that tawhid can only be proved by rational argument.
Correction: Sanusi notes that scriptural proof is also considered valid by many, though some scholars object.

Checkpoint
Why did some scholars reject using scriptural proof for tawhid?
A) Because scriptural proofs are unreliable
B) Because the existence of the Creator cannot be established by scripture, and tawhid depends on that existence
C) Because they were rationalists
D) Because the Quran does not mention tawhid

Key Takeaway
Scriptural proof for divine unity is valid, but some scholars argue that it cannot establish tawhid because it cannot establish the existence of the Creator, which is a prerequisite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek to strengthen your belief in Tawhid, do you rely more on rational arguments or on the Quran and Sunnah? What does that reveal about your understanding of how faith is built?
?
If scriptural proof alone cannot establish the existence of the Creator, as some scholars argue, what does that imply about the role of your intellect in your faith? Have you taken the time to rationally affirm the existence of God, or do you take it for granted?
?
The muḥaqqiqūn's objection challenges the very foundation of using scripture to prove Tawhid. Does this make you uneasy? Why? What is the ultimate source of your certainty in Tawhid?

Reason’s Inability to Know Good and Evil Without Revelation
Sanusi concludes that reason cannot discern good and evil without revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why reason alone cannot determine moral good and evil without revelation, and why revelation is necessary.

Core Idea
Reason without revelation leads to moral uncertainty.

Sanusi concludes his argument by reminding us that without revelation, reason is lost in conflicting possibilities. He shows that those who claim reason can independently know good and evil are mistaken, and even if we grant their premise, reason cannot settle the matter.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise of no preference
Sanusi starts with the key point: no action is inherently preferred over another from God's perspective. This sets the stage.
2
Identify the faulty claim
Then he identifies the error of those who think reason can know good and evil without revelation.
3
Consider the hypothetical counterargument
Even if we allow their claim, reason cannot reach certainty due to conflicting aspects of reasoning.
This shows the limitation of reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت بما ذكر عدم رجحان بعض الأفعال على بعض بالنسبة إليه تعالى.. عرفت جهالة من تسور على الغيب، ورأى أن العقل يتوصل دون شرع إلى إدراك الحسن والقبيح عنده جل وعلا، على أنه لو سلم لهم ذلك جدلاً لم يجزم العقل بشيء؛ لتعارض أوجُهِ من النظر في ذلك متضادة
And if you know, by what has been mentioned, the absence of preference of some actions over others with respect to Him, the Exalted, then you know the ignorance of one who intrudes upon the unseen, and thinks that reason can, without revelation, arrive at the perception of good and evil in His sight, the Mighty and Majestic. And even if that were granted to them hypothetically, reason could not be certain of anything due to the conflict of aspects of inquiry that are contradictory.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a concluding argument. It first restates the key point established earlier: there is no preference (rujhan) of some actions over others from God's perspective. From this, one can recognize the ignorance of those who claim that reason ('aql) can independently know good (husn) and evil (qubh) without revelation (shar'). The text adds that even if such a claim were granted for argument's sake, reason would still be unable to make a definitive judgment because different aspects of reasoning conflict with each other. Thus, revelation is necessary for moral knowledge.

The Four Sources of Islamic Rulings
Sanusi lists the four usul and emphasizes following the Salaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four foundational sources of Islamic rulings and the importance of following the Salaf as the path to salvation.

Core Idea
Islamic rulings derive from Quran, Sunnah, consensus, and analogy; following the Salaf ensures salvation.

Imam Sanusi begins his methodological foundation by identifying the four primary sources of Islamic law. He then immediately links these sources to the practice of the pious predecessors (Salaf), indicating that true adherence to the sources is embodied in their way. This short passage sets the stage for all subsequent theological and legal reasoning in the Aqeedah course.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation, to get a feel for the structure. Notice how Sanusi lists the sources concisely.
The phrase 'به' may be ambiguous; the commentary clarifies it refers to following the Salaf.
2
Identify the four sources
Explicitly list the four sources: the Book, the Sunnah, the consensus of the community, and the analogy of the imams. Understand each term's basic meaning.
These are the agreed-upon usul among Sunni scholars.
3
Understand the role of the Salaf
Note that Sanusi says following the Salaf is 'salvation for whoever clings to it'. Reflect on why he connects sources and Salaf.
The Salaf are the premier example of how to use the sources correctly.
4
Reflect on the connection
Ask yourself: Why does Sanusi mention the Salaf right after the sources? What does this teach about the methodology of acquiring knowledge?
This shows that proper understanding of the sources is inseparable from the Salaf's guidance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أنَّ أصول الأحكام التي منها نتلقى : الكتاب، والسنَّةُ، وإجماع الأمة، وقياس الأئمة .. به واتباع السلف الصالح واقتفاء آثَارِهِم نجاةٌ لَمَنْ تمسَّكَ وأفضلُ الناس بعد نبينا صلى الله عليهِ وسلَّمَ : أبو بكرٍ، ثم عمرُ، ومختارُ مالكِ : الوقفُ فيما بينَ عثمانَ وعلي رضيَ الله تعالى عنهما وعمَّنْ قبلهما والصحابة كلهم عدول أئمة، بأيهم اقتديتم اهتديتم، نفعنا الله بمحبَّتِهم، وأماتنا على سُنَّتِهم، وحشرنا في
And know: that the foundations of the rulings (uṣūl al-aḥkām) from which we derive are: the Book, the Sunnah, the consensus of the community (ijmāʿ al-umma), and the analogy of the imams (qiyās al-aʾimma). And following the pious predecessors (al-salaf al-ṣāliḥ) and tracing their footsteps is salvation for whoever clings to it. And the best of people after our Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) is Abū Bakr, then ʿUmar. And the chosen view of Mālik is to refrain (al-waqf) regarding what happened between ʿUthmān and ʿAlī (may God be pleased with them and with those before them). And all the Companions are just (ʿudūl), imams. Whichever of them you take as a guide, you will be guided. May God benefit us with love for them, cause us to die upon their way (sunnatihim), and resurrect us among...
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a concise statement of Sunni theological and legal principles. It begins by listing the four foundations (uṣūl) of Islamic rulings: the Quran, the Sunnah, consensus (ijmāʿ) of the community, and analogy (qiyās) of the imams. Then it emphasizes that following the pious predecessors (al-salaf al-ṣāliḥ) and imitating their path is salvation for whoever holds fast to it. Next, it addresses the hierarchy of the best people after the Prophet: the most excellent is Abū Bakr, then ʿUmar. Regarding the disputes between ʿUthmān and ʿAlī, it mentions that the chosen view of Imām Mālik (raḥimahullāh) is to refrain (al-waqf) from taking sides or delving into what happened. The passage then affirms that all the Companions are just (ʿudūl) and are leaders (aʾimmah); whoever follows any of them will be guided. The author concludes with a supplication that Allah benefit us through love for them, cause us to die upon their way, and resurrect us among them (the sentence is incomplete due to the page ending).

Key Takeaway

The Virtue of the Companions and Imam Malik's Stance
Sanusi ranks the best Companions, states Imam Malik's position on the disputes, and affirms the justness of all Companions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hierarchy of the best Companions after the Prophet, Imam Malik's view on the disputes between 'Uthman and 'Ali, and the justness of all Companions.

Core Idea
The best after the Prophet are Abu Bakr then Umar; all Companions are just and following any leads to guidance.

After establishing the sources and the importance of the Salaf, Sanusi turns to the individuals who constitute the Salaf—the Companions. He affirms the Sunni consensus on the superiority of Abu Bakr and Umar, highlights the proper adab regarding the conflicts among later Companions by mentioning Imam Malik's position of refraining, and underscores the comprehensive justness of all Companions. This passage safeguards against sectarian excesses and anchors love for the Prophet's household.

Guided Path
1

This is a point of creed regarding the superiority of the two shaykhs.
3

Sanusi mentions that the chosen view of Imam Malik is 'al-waqf' regarding the conflicts between 'Uthman and 'Ali – meaning to refrain from taking sides or passing judgment. This reflects a stance of respect and caution.

Nazar Does Not Depend on Knowledge of Necessity
Sanusi refutes that nazar depends on knowledge of logical necessity, customarily and legally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the obligation to engage in speculative reflection (nazar) does not require prior knowledge of logical necessity, but rather depends on the ability to know.

Core Idea
You don't need to know logic to be obligated to reflect on creation.

Sanusi tackles a common objection: that one must first understand logical categories before being obligated to engage in theological reflection. He argues that this is false, both from how people naturally behave and from the legal conditions of obligation. This lesson unpacks his argument, showing that the first duty is simply to use your God-given intellect to reflect on the marvels of creation and the message of the prophets.

Guided Path
1
State the thesis
Read Sanusi's opening claim that nazar is not dependent on knowledge of logical necessity, neither customarily nor legally.
This is the core position being defended.
2
Understand the customarily proof
Sanusi argues that God's normal pattern is that rational people naturally reflect on wonders; they don't first need a logical concept.
This shows nazar is instinctive, not taught.
3
Understand the legal proof
Legally, obligation depends on ability to know (having intellect, maturity, and receiving the message), not on actual knowledge.
The ability to know is enough.
4
Identify the conditions for obligation
Sanusi lists three conditions that make nazar obligatory: sound intellect, reaching puberty, and receiving the prophetic call in a way that demands reflection.
These are the only prerequisites.
5
Grasp the conclusion
The first obligation is nazar, not prior knowledge of logical categories.
This refutes the opposing view completely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحقُ : أنَّ النظر لا يتوقَّفُ على العلـم بـالـوجـوب لا عادةً ولا شرعاً : أَمَّا عادةً : فلأنَّ الله تعالى أجرى عادتَهُ وطردَ سُنتَهُ بعدم تواطؤ العقلاء على الإعراض عن النظر في عجائب الكائنات وغرائب المصنوعات، ومن أعظم ذلك ما تأتي بهِ الرُّسُلُ مِنْ خوارق العاداتِ . وأما شرعاً : فلأنَّ النظر وجوبُهُ مُتوقف على التمكَّنِ مِنَ العلم، لا على العلم) وقوله : ( أنْ يُعمِلَ فكرَهُ ( خبرُ ( أنَّ ) ، وحاصلُهُ : أَنَّ أَوَّلَ واجب النظر (1) والتمكن حاصل : بثبوت العقل، والبلوغ، وبلوغ الدعوة بلوغاً يدعو للنظر
And the truth is that nazar (reflection) is not dependent on knowledge of [logical] necessity, neither customarily nor legally. As for customarily: because God Almighty has established His custom and enforced His sunna by the lack of consensus of rational people to turn away from reflection on the wonders of existents and the marvels of created things, and among the greatest of that is what the messengers bring of miracles. And as for legally: because the obligation of nazar is dependent on the ability to know, not on knowledge. And His statement: 'that he exercises his thought' is the predicate of 'that', and its gist: that the first obligation is nazar. (1) And the ability is attained by the possession of intellect, reaching puberty, and the arrival of the call in a manner that calls for reflection.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opposing view. Sanusi states his thesis: nazar (speculative reflection) does not depend on prior knowledge of the concept of logical necessity. He proves this in two ways: (1) Customarily, God's normal pattern is that rational people have never unanimously agreed to ignore reflecting upon the wonders of creation, including the miracles of prophets. Thus, nazar is naturally prompted without needing abstract knowledge of necessity. (2) Legally, the obligation of nazar is conditional only on the ability to know (i.e., having intellect, maturity, and hearing the prophetic message), not on actual knowledge of logical categories. Then he clarifies that the first thing a person is obligated to do is to engage in this reflection (nazar), and that the ability for it is realized by three conditions: sound intellect, reaching puberty, and receiving the prophetic call in a way that demands response.

Key Terms
النظر
الوجوب
التمكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One must first master logic and know the concept of logical necessity before being obligated to engage in theological reflection.
Correction: Sanusi argues that nazar is naturally prompted and legally depends only on ability to know, not prior knowledge of logical necessity.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the first thing a person is obligated to do?
A) Know the logical categories of necessary, possible, and impossible
B) Engage in nazar (reflection) on creation
C) Accept the Shahada without reflection
D) Study the works of al-Ash'ari

Key Takeaway
The obligation to reflect on creation and the prophetic message does not require prior knowledge of logical categories; it only requires the capacity to know.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever delayed reflecting on the purpose of your existence because you felt 'not ready' or 'not knowledgeable enough'? What would it mean to hold yourself accountable starting now, with nothing but your intellect?
?
When you hear the call of the Prophet (peace be upon him) through the Quran or sunnah, do you feel a compelling need to think deeply, or do you brush it aside? What would change in your daily life if you treated that call as immediately binding on you?
?
Look at the sky, your own body, or any everyday sign of God. When was the last time you truly reflected on it as a sign? What would it cost you to pause for one minute today and think with the intention of knowing your Creator?

Definition of Hujja and Dalil with a Simple Example
Sanusi defines hujja/dalil and gives first example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of hujja and dalil as knowledge of relating one thing to another affirmatively or negatively, and see how a definition exemplifies this.

Core Idea
Hujja and dalil are not just proofs; they are any relational knowledge.

Sanusi begins by defining the key terms 'hujja' and 'dalil' which are central to Islamic reasoning. He gives a precise conceptual definition and then illustrates it with a straightforward example from logic and ontology. This lesson examines the definition and its first example.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then its translation. Notice that it starts with a definition and then gives an example.
2
Understand the definition
Focus on the phrase 'العلم بنسبة أمرٍ إلى أمرٍ على جهة الثبوتِ أو النفي'. This means knowledge of the relation between two things, either affirming or denying a predicate.
The term 'hujja' and 'dalil' are used interchangeably here.
3
Analyze the first example
The example is 'your saying in explaining man: he is the rational animal'. Identify the two things related: man and rational animal. The relation is affirmation.
This is a definition (sharḥ) which serves as a hujja.
4
Reflect on the significance
Think about why a simple definition qualifies as hujja. It provides certain knowledge about man's essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو العلم بنسبة أمرٍ إلى أمرٍ على جهة الثبوتِ أو النفي .. سُمِّيَتْ حُجَّةً ودليلاً . فمثالُ الأَوَّلِ : قولُكَ في شرح الإنسانِ : إِنَّهُ الحيوانُ الناطق . ومثال الثاني : قولُكَ في بيان حدوثِ العالم ـ وهو ما سوى الله جلَّ وعلا - : : العالمُ مُتغيّر ، وكلُّ مُتغيّر حادث ، فإنَّ ترتيب هاتين القضيتين المعلومتين على الوجه الخاص ؛ وهو كون الصغرى موجبة ، والكبرى كليَّةٌ .. يوصلُ مَنِ اتَّضحَ له بالبرهان صدقهما إلى العلم بأنَّ العالم حادث ؛ لاندراج الصغرى في حكم الكبرى
It is knowledge of relating one thing to another in terms of affirmation or negation.. it is called hujja and dalil. An example of the first: your saying in explaining man: he is the rational animal. An example of the second: your saying in explaining the temporality of the world—which is everything besides Allah, exalted and majestic—: the world is changing, everything changing is temporal; for the arrangement of these two known propositions in the specific manner—which is the minor premise being affirmative and the major premise universal—leads, for the one for whom their truth is evident by proof, to knowledge that the world is temporal, because the minor premise is subsumed under the ruling of the major premise.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage defines the terms 'hujja' and 'dalil' (proof and evidence) and illustrates their application with two examples. The definition states that hujja/dalil is knowledge (al-ilm) of the relation (nisba) between one thing and another, in terms of affirmation (thubūt) or negation (nafy). The author then gives two examples. The first example is a statement that explains the essence of man: 'he is the rational animal.' This is a definition (sharḥ) that affirms a predicate (nāṭiq) of a subject (insān). The second example is a proof (bayān) for the temporality (ḥudūth) of the world. It is a syllogism: 'The world is changing; everything changing is temporal.' The author explains that arranging these two known propositions in a specific manner—the minor premise (ṣughrā) being affirmative and the major premise (kubrā) universal—leads anyone who has grasped their truth through demonstration (burhān) to the knowledge that the world is temporal, because the minor premise is included under the ruling of the major premise (indirāj al-ṣughrā fī ḥukm al-kubrā). This shows that hujja/dalil can take the form of both a definition and a deductive proof.

Key Terms
حجة
دليل
نسبة
ثبوت
نفي
شرح
إنسان
ناطق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think hujja and dalil only refer to complex syllogisms.
Correction: Sanusi shows that even a simple definition like 'man is the rational animal' qualifies as a hujja.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is an example of hujja/dalil?
A) The world is changing
B) Man is the rational animal
C) Everything changing is temporal
D) The minor premise

Key Takeaway
Hujja and dalil are defined as knowledge of relating one thing to another, either affirmatively or negatively, and a simple definition like 'man is the rational animal' serves as an example.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe your faith to others, do you use clear definitions that link concepts precisely, or do you rely on vague impressions? How might that affect the strength of your testimony?
?
If every definition you hold about Allah and His attributes is a hujja (proof) that either affirms or negates something, which of your definitions most urgently needs correction or refinement?
?
Think of a time you misunderstood a religious text because of an unclear definition. How would taking this lesson—that definitions are proofs—change how you approach that text today?

The Second Example: A Syllogistic Proof for the Temporality of the World
Sanusi gives second example: syllogistic proof for world's temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a syllogistic argument for the temporality of the world exemplifies hujja and dalil, particularly through the arrangement of premises.

Core Idea
A hujja can be a syllogism that leads from known premises to a new conclusion.

After giving a simple definition as an example, Sanusi now presents a more complex example: a logical proof that the world is temporal. This illustrates hujja as a syllogism with two premises arranged in a specific manner. This lesson unpacks that argument and its justification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for this example
Read the Arabic sentences for the second example and its translation. Notice it consists of two premises and a conclusion mechanism.
2
Identify the two premises
The premises are: 'The world is changing' and 'Everything changing is temporal'. These are known propositions.
The world here means everything besides Allah.
3
Understand the specific arrangement
Sanusi says the arrangement is specific: the minor premise (صغرى) is affirmative and the major premise (كبرى) is universal. This arrangement is necessary for the conclusion to follow.
This is a valid syllogistic form.
4
See how the conclusion is reached
The conclusion that the world is temporal is reached because the minor premise falls under the universal ruling of the major premise (indirāj). This yields certain knowledge.
The mechanism is subsumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو العلم بنسبة أمرٍ إلى أمرٍ على جهة الثبوتِ أو النفي .. سُمِّيَتْ حُجَّةً ودليلاً . فمثالُ الأَوَّلِ : قولُكَ في شرح الإنسانِ : إِنَّهُ الحيوانُ الناطق . ومثال الثاني : قولُكَ في بيان حدوثِ العالم ـ وهو ما سوى الله جلَّ وعلا - : : العالمُ مُتغيّر ، وكلُّ مُتغيّر حادث ، فإنَّ ترتيب هاتين القضيتين المعلومتين على الوجه الخاص ؛ وهو كون الصغرى موجبة ، والكبرى كليَّةٌ .. يوصلُ مَنِ اتَّضحَ له بالبرهان صدقهما إلى العلم بأنَّ العالم حادث ؛ لاندراج الصغرى في حكم الكبرى
It is knowledge of relating one thing to another in terms of affirmation or negation.. it is called hujja and dalil. An example of the first: your saying in explaining man: he is the rational animal. An example of the second: your saying in explaining the temporality of the world—which is everything besides Allah, exalted and majestic—: the world is changing, everything changing is temporal; for the arrangement of these two known propositions in the specific manner—which is the minor premise being affirmative and the major premise universal—leads, for the one for whom their truth is evident by proof, to knowledge that the world is temporal, because the minor premise is subsumed under the ruling of the major premise.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage defines the terms 'hujja' and 'dalil' (proof and evidence) and illustrates their application with two examples. The definition states that hujja/dalil is knowledge (al-ilm) of the relation (nisba) between one thing and another, in terms of affirmation (thubūt) or negation (nafy). The author then gives two examples. The first example is a statement that explains the essence of man: 'he is the rational animal.' This is a definition (sharḥ) that affirms a predicate (nāṭiq) of a subject (insān). The second example is a proof (bayān) for the temporality (ḥudūth) of the world. It is a syllogism: 'The world is changing; everything changing is temporal.' The author explains that arranging these two known propositions in a specific manner—the minor premise (ṣughrā) being affirmative and the major premise (kubrā) universal—leads anyone who has grasped their truth through demonstration (burhān) to the knowledge that the world is temporal, because the minor premise is included under the ruling of the major premise (indirāj al-ṣughrā fī ḥukm al-kubrā). This shows that hujja/dalil can take the form of both a definition and a deductive proof.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the conclusion is directly stated without reasoning.
Correction: Sanusi emphasizes the arrangement of premises and the logical subsumption as the cause of knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is necessary for the syllogism to lead to knowledge?
A) The premises must be unknown
B) The minor premise must be universal
C) The minor premise must be affirmative and the major universal
D) The conclusion must be stated clearly

Key Takeaway
The second example shows that a hujja can be a syllogistic proof with properly arranged premises, leading to knowledge of a new truth through subsumption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the world is changing and everything changing is temporal, then everything you own, every relationship, and even your own life is temporal. How does this truth change what you will do today? Name one attachment you must consciously let go of.
?
The premises of this proof must be known by demonstration (burhān). Are you certain that the world is changing? Look at your own fleeting thoughts and feelings: is there any stability in you? What does this certainty demand from your heart?
?
If you truly internalized that the world is temporal, would you live differently right now? What is one specific change in your daily routine that would reflect the belief that this world is passing away—a change you will commit to starting today?

Four Schools on the Connection Between Proof and Result
Sanusi enumerates four madhahib on the relationship between proof and result.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four madhahib on the nature of the connection between a proof and its result, and which one is considered correct.

Core Idea
The link between proof and result is not self-evident; four schools offer different explanations.

Sanusi presents a key methodological question: what kind of relationship holds between a proof (dalīl) and its conclusion (natīja)? Is it merely habitual, intellectually necessary, or mediated by some causal process? He lists four schools of thought and indicates which one is correct.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi poses the question and then lists the schools.
Focus on the four alternatives in the opening question.
2
Identify the four madhahib
Find each of the four schools mentioned: al-Ash'ari, Imam al-Haramayn, the Mu'tazila, and the philosophers. For each, note their position on the connection.
Sanusi explicitly states that Imam al-Haramayn's position is correct.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila exception
The Mu'tazila generally hold tawallud (generation), but they make an exception for recollective consideration (al-naẓar al-tadhakkurī), agreeing with Imam al-Haramayn on that.
4
Note the refutation foreshadowed
Sanusi says the refutation of the last two madhahib (Mu'tazila and philosophers) will come later. This sets up the need to attribute all events directly to Allah.
This connects to a central tenet of Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهل الربط بين الدليل والنتيجة عادي فيمكن تخلفه ، أو عقلي فلا يمكن عندَ نفْيِ الآفاتِ العامَّةِ كالموتِ ونحوِه التخلُّفُ ، أو بالتولد ؛ بمعنى : أنَّ القدرة الحادثةَ أَثَّرَتْ في وجودِ النتيجة بواسطة تأثيرها في النظر ، أو بالإيجاب ؛ بمعنى : أنَّ النظرَ عِلَّةٌ أَثَرَتْ في وجودِ المعلول ؟ أربعة مذاهب : الأَوَّلُ : مذهب الأشعري ، والثاني : مذهب إمام الحرمين ، وهو الصحيح ، وللقاضي القولانِ ، والثالثُ : مذهب المعتزلة ، واستثنوا مِنْ ذلكَ النظر التذكُّري ، فقالوا فيه بقول الإمام ؛ لأنَّه كالنظر الذكري الضروري ، والرابع : مذهب الحكماء ، والردُّ على الأخيرين بما يأتي مِنْ وجوب إسنادِ وقوع الممكناتِ كلها إلى الله تعالى ابتداءً ، وإبطال أصل التولدِ والتعليل على سبيل التأثير
Is the connection between the proof and the result habitual, such that it can be absent, or intellectual such that absence is impossible when general defects like death and the like are negated, or by way of generation (tawallud), meaning that the originated power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) effects the existence of the result by means of its effecting on the consideration (al-naẓar), or by way of necessitation (ījāb), meaning that consideration is a cause that effects the existence of the effect? Four schools: the first is the school of al-Ash'ari; the second is the school of Imam al-Haramayn, which is correct; and for al-Qāḍī there are two sayings. The third is the school of the Mu'tazila, and they excepted from that the recollective consideration (al-naẓar al-tadhakkurī), saying about it according to the saying of the Imam [al-Haramayn] because it is like necessary recollective consideration. The fourth is the school of the philosophers. The refutation of the last two will come by way of the obligation of attributing the occurrence of all possible things to Allah initially, and the invalidation of the principle of generation and causal influence by way of effecting.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents four schools of thought (madhāhib) regarding the nature of the connection (rabṭ) between a proof (dalīl) and its result (natīja). The author first poses a question: Is the connection habitual (ʿādī) such that it could be absent, or intellectual (ʿaqlī) such that it cannot be absent when general defects like death are absent, or by generation (tawallud) meaning that the originated power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) influences the result through its influence on consideration (naẓar), or by necessitation (ījāb) meaning that consideration is a cause that produces the effect? He then lists the four positions: (1) The school of al-Ashʿarī – connection is habitual; (2) The school of Imām al-Ḥaramayn – it is intellectual, and this is correct; al-Qāḍī has two opinions; (3) The school of the Muʿtazila – it is by generation, but they except recollective consideration (al-naẓar al-tadhakkurī) and agree with Imām al-Ḥaramayn on that; (4) The school of the philosophers – it is by necessitation. The author notes that the refutation of the last two will come later, based on the obligation of attributing all possible events to Allah directly and invalidating the principles of generation and causal influence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Ash'ari position is the correct one because it is from the founder of the school.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly states that the correct position is that of Imam al-Haramayn, not al-Ash'ari.

Checkpoint
Which madhhab does Sanusi identify as the correct one regarding the relationship between proof and result?
A) The Ash'ari
B) Imam al-Haramayn
C) The Mu'tazila
D) The philosophers

Key Takeaway
There are four schools on the connection between proof and result; Imam al-Haramayn's intellectual connection is the correct one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the four views on the connection between proof and result actually describes your own experience when you reflect on the proofs of tawhid? Is your conviction merely habitual, or is it intellectually necessary? If it is habitual, what happens when that habit is shaken by doubts or trials? Will you seek to make your connection intellectual by deepening your understanding?
?
The Mu'tazila made an exception for recollective consideration—when you think back on Allah's signs, do you find that your heart automatically connects the proof to the result, or has your consideration become stale and disconnected? This lesson calls you to examine the quality of your naẓar. Will you renew your consideration today so that it yields unbreakable certainty?
?
Imam al-Haramayn's view—that the connection is intellectual and cannot fail when general defects are absent—is declared correct. What 'general defects' (like laziness, distraction, or heedlessness) might be present in your own consideration? Will you remove these defects to ensure that your proofs always lead to the result of firm faith?

Refuting the Deniers of Nazar's Efficacy
Sanusi refutes deniers of nazar and answers an objection about necessary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's refutation of those who deny nazar's efficacy and his distinction between necessary knowledge with and without a cause.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge with a cause requires sharing that cause.

Sanusi confronts two groups that reject the cognitive power of rational consideration (nazar). He declares their error obvious and then defends the necessity of nazar against a subtle objection. This passage teaches us that not all necessary knowledge is universal; some depends on experiencing its cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups
The Sumaniyya deny nazar absolutely; the Muhandisin deny it in divine matters. Understand who they are.
2
Understand the refutation
Sanusi says their corruption is clear and that experiential knowledge suffices to refute them.
3
Analyze the objection and answer
The objection claims necessary knowledge cannot be disputed; the answer distinguishes between types of necessary knowledge.
Notice the example of the whole being greater than its part vs. sweetness requiring taste.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما مذهبُ السُّمَنِيَّةِ المانعينَ إفادة النظر مطلقاً ، والمهندسين المانعينَ إفادته في الإلهياتِ : فلا يخفى فسادهما ، وضرورة العلم بإفادتِهِ المستفادةُ مِنَ التجربة . . كافية في الردّ عليهما فيه لا يُقالُ : الضروريُّ لا يختلفُ فيهِ العقلاء ، وهذا قد اختلفوا لأنَّا نقولُ : ذلك في الضروري الذي ليس له سببٌ ؛ ككونِ الكلِّ أعظم مِنْ جزئهِ ، أما ما له سبب كهذا فلا يدركُهُ ضرورةٌ إلا مَنْ شارك في السبب ؛ كحلاوة هذا الطعام مثلاً ، لا يدركه ضرورةٌ إلا مَنْ شارك في سببه الذي هو الذوقُ ، والسبب في مسألتنا : العثور على
As for the school of the Sumaniyya who deny the efficacy of consideration absolutely, and the engineers (Muhandisin) who deny its efficacy in divine matters: their corruption is not hidden, and the necessity of knowledge of its efficacy derived from experience is sufficient to refute both of them. It cannot be said: the necessary (knowledge) is not differed about by the rational people, and these people have differed. Because we say: that is regarding the necessary which has no cause, like the whole being greater than its part. As for that which has a cause like this, it is only apprehended necessarily by one who shares in the cause, like the sweetness of this food, for example, it is not apprehended necessarily except by one who shares in its cause which is taste. And the cause in our case is: finding (al-ʿuthūr ʿalā).
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage refutes two groups that deny the efficacy of consideration (naẓar) in producing knowledge. The Sumaniyya deny it entirely, while the Muhandisin (engineers/geometers) deny it in divine matters (ilāhiyyāt). The author declares that the corruption of their positions is obvious, and that the necessary knowledge of the efficacy of consideration, derived from experience, suffices to refute them. The passage then addresses a potential objection: someone might say that necessary knowledge (ḍarūrī) is not subject to disagreement among rational people, yet these groups have disagreed, so how can the efficacy be necessary? The answer distinguishes two types of necessary knowledge. The first type has no cause (sabab), like the knowledge that the whole is greater than its part; such knowledge is universally agreed upon. The second type has a cause, like the knowledge of sweetness, which is only necessarily known by those who share in the cause (i.e., taste). In the matter of consideration, the cause is 'finding' (al-ʿuthūr ʿalā), though the text cuts off here. Thus, disagreement arises from a difference in experiencing the cause, not from the absence of necessity.

مهندسين
إفادة النظر
ضروري
سبب
ذوق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessary knowledge is always universally agreed upon.
Correction: Sanusi explains that necessary knowledge that has a cause is only perceived by those who share that cause, so disagreement can arise from differing experiences.

Checkpoint
How does Sanusi answer the objection that necessary knowledge cannot be subject to disagreement?
A) He denies the disagreement exists
B) He distinguishes between necessary knowledge with a cause and without
C) He says the objectors are not rational
D) He says the cause is unknown

Key Takeaway
Necessary knowledge can be of two kinds: one without a cause that is universal, and one with a cause that requires sharing the cause to be perceived necessarily.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a deep question about your Creator, do you earnestly seek the cause (ʿuthūr) that leads to necessary conviction, or do you rely on comfortable assumptions? What has your actual practice been this past week?
?
Are you willing to admit that some of your disagreements with others about fundamental truths may stem from a lack of shared experience or sincere pursuit, not from the absence of truth? How will you change your approach starting today?
?
If the sweetness of food requires taste to be known necessarily, what does that imply about your need to purify your heart and intentions to perceive the proofs of Allah? What concrete step will you take to seek that purification?

Refutation by Experience and the Two Types of Necessary Knowledge
Sanusi refutes the denial of nazar's benefit using experience and a distinction on necessary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's initial refutation of those who deny that nazar yields knowledge, and his key distinction between necessary knowledge with a cause and necessary knowledge without a cause.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge from nazar has a cause; only those who share the cause perceive it.

Sanusi opens his refutation by asserting that the corruption of the opposing views is obvious, and that the necessity of knowledge derived from experience is sufficient. He then preempts an objection about disagreement among rational people by distinguishing two types of necessary knowledge. This distinction is crucial for understanding why not everyone agrees on the benefits of nazar.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening refutation
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where Sanusi states that the doctrines of the Sumaniyya and geometricians are clearly corrupt, and that the necessity of knowledge from experience suffices against them.
Notice the confidence: 'لا يخفى فسادهما' (their corruption is not hidden).
2
Identify the objection
Find the phrase 'لا يقال' (it is not said) — this signals an objection. Understand why someone might say that necessary knowledge cannot be disagreed upon.
3
Understand the distinction
Study Sanusi's answer: there are two types of necessary knowledge — one without a cause (e.g., whole greater than part) and one with a cause (e.g., sweetness of food). The knowledge from nazar has a cause, so it is only necessary for those who share that cause.
The cause here is finding correct nazar that reveals the aspect of the proof.
4
Apply the analogy
Reflect on the analogy of tasting sweetness: only those who taste know the sweetness necessarily. Similarly, only those who engage in correct nazar will necessarily know its benefit.
This shows why the opponents do not share that necessary knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرد على المانعين من إفادة النظر مطلقا أو في الإلهيات وأما مذهب السمنية المانعين إفادة النظر مطلقا والمهندسين المانعين إفادته في الإلهيات فلا يخفى فسادهما وضرورة العلم بإفادته المستفادة من التجربة كافية في الرد عليهما فيه لا يقال الضروري لا يختلف فيه العقلاء وهذا قد اختلفوا لأنا نقول ذلك في الضروري الذي ليس له سبب ككون الكل أعظم من جزئه أما ما له سبب كهذا فلا يدركه ضرورة إلا من شارك في السبب كحلاوة هذا الطعام مثلا لا يدركه ضرورة إلا من شارك في سببه الذي هو الذوق والسبب في مسألتنا العثور على النظر الصحيح المطلع على وجه الدليل وما احتج به المهندسون من أن الحكم على الشيء فرع تصوره وحقيقة الإله يستحيل تصورها فلا يدرك بالنظر الحكم عليها وبأن أقرب الأشياء إلى الإنسان هويته التي يشير إليها بأنا وفيها من كثرة الخلاف ما علم فما ظنك بأبعدها عن الأوهام والعقول فممنوع أما الأول فلان الحكم إنما يتوقف على تصور ما هو موجود لا على كمال التصور وأما الثاني فلا ينتج الامتناع بل العسر وهو مسلم لا شك فيه إذ الوهم يلابس العقل في مأخذه والباطل يشاكل الحق في مباحثه ولهذا كان أهل الحق في غاية القلة ومنع أن يخوض فيما زاد على الضروري من هذا العلم إلا الأفراد من الأذكياء
The refutation of those who prevent [the idea] that theoretical reflection yields knowledge absolutely or in divine matters. As for the doctrine of the Sumaniyya, who prevent that theoretical reflection yields knowledge absolutely, and the geometricians, who prevent that it yields [knowledge] in divine matters: their corruption is not hidden, and the necessity of knowledge of its benefit derived from experience is sufficient in refuting them. With respect to it, it is not said: necessary [knowledge] does not differ among rational people, and these people have differed [about it]. Because we say: that [applies] to the necessary that has no cause, like the whole being greater than its part. As for what has a cause like this, it is not perceived by necessity except by one who participates in the cause, like the sweetness of this food, for example; it is not perceived by necessity except by one who participates in its cause, which is taste. And the cause in our issue is coming upon correct theoretical reflection that reveals the aspect of the proof. And what the geometricians adduced, that judgment about a thing is a branch of its conception, and the reality of the divine is impossible to conceive, so judgment about it cannot be perceived by theoretical reflection, and that the nearest thing to man is his essence which he indicates by 'I', and in it there is much disagreement as is known, so what do you think about that which is furthest from imaginations and intellects? It is rejected. As for the first: because judgment only depends on conceiving what is existent, not on perfect conception. And as for the second: it does not produce impossibility, but only difficulty, and that is conceded without doubt, because imagination mingles with intellect in its method, and falsehood resembles truth in its investigations. For this reason the people of truth are extremely few, and it is forbidden to delve beyond the necessary in this science except the exceptional among the intelligent.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two groups who deny that theoretical reflection (nazar) yields knowledge: the Sumaniyya, who deny it absolutely, and the geometricians (Muhandisin), who deny it only in divine matters. Sanusi first asserts that the corruption of their positions is obvious, and the necessity of knowledge that nazar is beneficial, derived from experience, is sufficient refutation. Then he addresses an objection: it is said that necessary knowledge does not differ among rational people, yet these people differ about this knowledge. The answer distinguishes two types of necessary knowledge: one without a cause (like the whole being greater than its part), which is universally agreed upon, and one with a cause (like the sweetness of food), which is only perceived by necessity by those who share the cause (taste). The cause in our question is finding the correct nazar that reveals the aspect of the proof. Next, Sanusi presents the arguments of the geometricians: first, that judgment about a thing depends on its conception, and the divine essence is impossible to conceive, so judgment cannot be obtained; second, that the closest thing to man is his own essence, yet there is much disagreement about it, so how about what is furthest? Sanusi refutes: for the first, judgment only depends on conceiving the existence of the thing, not perfect conception; for the second, the argument does not prove impossibility but only difficulty, which is conceded. Difficulty arises because imagination mingles with intellect and falsehood resembles truth. Hence the people of truth are few, and only exceptional intelligences may delve into this science beyond the necessary.

Key Terms
النظر
السمنية
المهندسون
الضروري
التجربة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if knowledge is necessary, it must be universally agreed upon by all rational people.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that necessary knowledge with a cause is only necessary for those who share the cause, so disagreement does not disprove its necessity.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why does the objection that necessary knowledge does not differ among rational people not apply to the knowledge gained through nazar?
A) Because nazar is not necessary knowledge.
B) Because necessary knowledge with a cause is only perceived by those who share the cause.
C) Because rational people actually agree about nazar.
D) Because the knowledge from nazar is not necessary but acquired.

Key Takeaway
Necessary knowledge from nazar is conditional on engaging in correct theoretical reflection; it is not universal like self-evident truths.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a truth about Allah or your faith simply because you didn't 'taste' it through experience or reflection? What cause might you need to pursue—through study, humility, or practice—to perceive that truth? This week, commit to one concrete act of seeking that cause.
?
When you encounter a difficult proof (like those in kalam), do you tend to think 'this is not for everyone' and stop engaging, or do you press into it to share in its cause? How does Sanusi's distinction challenge you to take responsibility for your own understanding?
?
Your heart readily affirms certain beliefs about Allah without proof, but your mind may resist others. Which specific truth of ‘aqidah have you accepted only passively, without the inner certainty that comes from correct reflection? What is one step you will take today to acquire that certainty?

Refuting the Geometricians' Arguments
Sanusi presents and refutes two arguments of the geometricians against nazar in divine matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two arguments of the geometricians against nazar in divine matters and Sanusi's refutations of each.

Core Idea
Judgment only requires conceiving existence, not perfect conception; difficulty is not impossibility.

The geometricians raised two sophisticated objections: first, that judgment depends on conception and the divine is inconceivable, so no knowledge can be gained; second, that even the nearest thing to us, our own essence, is disputed, so what of the furthest? Sanusi refutes both: judgment only needs conceiving the thing's existence, not its full essence, and the second argument only shows difficulty, not impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the geometricians' first argument
Read the part where they claim judgment depends on conception, and since the divine essence is impossible to conceive, no judgment can be obtained through nazar.
2
Study Sanusi's refutation of the first argument
Notice that Sanusi says judgment only requires conceiving what is existent, not perfect conception. That is, we need to know that God exists, not fully grasp His essence.
This preserves possibility of knowing attributes and actions.
3
Read the geometricians' second argument
They argue from disagreement about the nearest thing (our own essence) to the impossibility of knowing the furthest (the divine).
4
Understand Sanusi's refutation of the second argument
Sanusi says this only proves difficulty, not impossibility. Difficulty is due to imagination mixing with intellect and falsehood resembling truth, but it does not preclude knowledge for the intelligent few.
This leads to the conclusion that only exceptional people can delve into divine matters beyond the necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرد على المانعين من إفادة النظر مطلقا أو في الإلهيات وأما مذهب السمنية المانعين إفادة النظر مطلقا والمهندسين المانعين إفادته في الإلهيات فلا يخفى فسادهما وضرورة العلم بإفادته المستفادة من التجربة كافية في الرد عليهما فيه لا يقال الضروري لا يختلف فيه العقلاء وهذا قد اختلفوا لأنا نقول ذلك في الضروري الذي ليس له سبب ككون الكل أعظم من جزئه أما ما له سبب كهذا فلا يدركه ضرورة إلا من شارك في السبب كحلاوة هذا الطعام مثلا لا يدركه ضرورة إلا من شارك في سببه الذي هو الذوق والسبب في مسألتنا العثور على النظر الصحيح المطلع على وجه الدليل وما احتج به المهندسون من أن الحكم على الشيء فرع تصوره وحقيقة الإله يستحيل تصورها فلا يدرك بالنظر الحكم عليها وبأن أقرب الأشياء إلى الإنسان هويته التي يشير إليها بأنا وفيها من كثرة الخلاف ما علم فما ظنك بأبعدها عن الأوهام والعقول فممنوع أما الأول فلان الحكم إنما يتوقف على تصور ما هو موجود لا على كمال التصور وأما الثاني فلا ينتج الامتناع بل العسر وهو مسلم لا شك فيه إذ الوهم يلابس العقل في مأخذه والباطل يشاكل الحق في مباحثه ولهذا كان أهل الحق في غاية القلة ومنع أن يخوض فيما زاد على الضروري من هذا العلم إلا الأفراد من الأذكياء
The refutation of those who prevent [the idea] that theoretical reflection yields knowledge absolutely or in divine matters. As for the doctrine of the Sumaniyya, who prevent that theoretical reflection yields knowledge absolutely, and the geometricians, who prevent that it yields [knowledge] in divine matters: their corruption is not hidden, and the necessity of knowledge of its benefit derived from experience is sufficient in refuting them. With respect to it, it is not said: necessary [knowledge] does not differ among rational people, and these people have differed [about it]. Because we say: that [applies] to the necessary that has no cause, like the whole being greater than its part. As for what has a cause like this, it is not perceived by necessity except by one who participates in the cause, like the sweetness of this food, for example; it is not perceived by necessity except by one who participates in its cause, which is taste. And the cause in our issue is coming upon correct theoretical reflection that reveals the aspect of the proof. And what the geometricians adduced, that judgment about a thing is a branch of its conception, and the reality of the divine is impossible to conceive, so judgment about it cannot be perceived by theoretical reflection, and that the nearest thing to man is his essence which he indicates by 'I', and in it there is much disagreement as is known, so what do you think about that which is furthest from imaginations and intellects? It is rejected. As for the first: because judgment only depends on conceiving what is existent, not on perfect conception. And as for the second: it does not produce impossibility, but only difficulty, and that is conceded without doubt, because imagination mingles with intellect in its method, and falsehood resembles truth in its investigations. For this reason the people of truth are extremely few, and it is forbidden to delve beyond the necessary in this science except the exceptional among the intelligent.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two groups who deny that theoretical reflection (nazar) yields knowledge: the Sumaniyya, who deny it absolutely, and the geometricians (Muhandisin), who deny it only in divine matters. Sanusi first asserts that the corruption of their positions is obvious, and the necessity of knowledge that nazar is beneficial, derived from experience, is sufficient refutation. Then he addresses an objection: it is said that necessary knowledge does not differ among rational people, yet these people differ about this knowledge. The answer distinguishes two types of necessary knowledge: one without a cause (like the whole being greater than its part), which is universally agreed upon, and one with a cause (like the sweetness of food), which is only perceived by necessity by those who share the cause (taste). The cause in our question is finding the correct nazar that reveals the aspect of the proof. Next, Sanusi presents the arguments of the geometricians: first, that judgment about a thing depends on its conception, and the divine essence is impossible to conceive, so judgment cannot be obtained; second, that the closest thing to man is his own essence, yet there is much disagreement about it, so how about what is furthest? Sanusi refutes: for the first, judgment only depends on conceiving the existence of the thing, not perfect conception; for the second, the argument does not prove impossibility but only difficulty, which is conceded. Difficulty arises because imagination mingles with intellect and falsehood resembles truth. Hence the people of truth are few, and only exceptional intelligences may delve into this science beyond the necessary.

التصور
الحكم
الإلهيات

Key Takeaway
Difficulty and disagreement do not imply impossibility; correct nazar can yield knowledge even in the most difficult subjects for those who are qualified.

?

The Timing of Knowledge of the Conclusion
Sanusi presents the disagreement over whether knowledge of the conclusion follows or coincides with knowledge of the proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement among scholars about whether knowledge of the conclusion follows after knowledge of the proof or occurs simultaneously, and the related question of whether it is one knowledge or two.

Core Idea
Knowledge of the conclusion may follow the proof or coincide with it—and that affects how we understand the unity of knowledge.

This passage introduces a further nuance within the discussion of nazar (speculative reasoning). Having established that nazar yields knowledge, scholars now differ on the precise timing of that knowledge. Does the knowledge of the conclusion arrive after we grasp the proof, or does it come at the same moment? And based on that, is it a single act of knowledge or two? This question may seem subtle, but it bears on how we understand the structure of rational certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two questions Sanusi raises.
2
Identify the two disagreements
List the two points of disagreement mentioned: timing and unity of knowledge.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why scholars disagree on this point. The commentary suggests it affects proof construction and certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هل العلم بالنتيجة يعقب العلم بوجه الدليل ثم اختلف القائلون بإفادته هل العلم بالنتيجة يعقب العلم بوجه الدليل أم يحصل معه دفعة وعليه فهل بعلم واحد أم بعلمين فيه خلاف
Is knowledge of the conclusion subsequent to knowledge of the aspect of the proof? Then those who hold that [nazar] yields [knowledge] differed: does knowledge of the conclusion follow knowledge of the aspect of the proof, or does it occur with it at once? And based on that, does it involve one knowledge or two knowledges? There is disagreement about it.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage presents a further disagreement among those who affirm that nazar yields knowledge. After establishing that nazar yields knowledge, scholars differ on two issues. First: does knowledge of the conclusion (al-ilm bi al-natija) follow after knowledge of the aspect of the proof (al-ilm bi wajh al-dalil), or does it occur simultaneously with it? Second: based on that difference, is the resulting knowledge one single knowledge or two knowledges? The passage simply states that there is disagreement (fihi khilaf) without elaborating on the arguments for each side.

Key Terms

العلم بوجه الدليل
دفعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This is a trivial scholastic question with no real impact.
Correction: This question affects how proofs are constructed and the nature of certainty.

Checkpoint

B) Whether knowledge of the conclusion follows after knowledge of the proof or occurs simultaneously, and whether it is one knowledge or two.

D) Whether the knowledge is of the essence or of the attributes.

Key Takeaway
Scholars who affirm that nazar yields knowledge disagree on whether the conclusion is known after the proof or at the same time, and whether it is one or two knowledges.
