The First Obligation of the Mukallaf: Opinions and Preference
Sanusi presents and evaluates the different opinions on the first obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different scholarly opinions on the first obligation of a mukallaf and why al-Sanusi prefers the view that it is nazar (intellectual consideration).

Core Idea
The first obligation is nazar, because revelation repeatedly commands it.

In this passage, al-Sanusi presents a note on a key disagreement among theologians: what is the very first thing a morally accountable person must do? Several major scholars gave different answers. As you read, pay attention to how Sanusi both reconciles some views and ultimately chooses one based on the strength of textual evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Get an overview of the different opinions mentioned.
The Arabic contains several proper names and technical terms.
2
Identify the four opinions
List the four opinions: (1) al-Ash'ari: nazar, (2) al-Ustadh and al-Juwayni: intention to nazar, (3) al-Baqillani: first part of nazar, (4) another: knowledge. Notice who held which view.
Sanusi attributes one view to al-Ash'ari in two different framings.
3
Understand Sanusi's reconciliation
Sanusi harmonizes the view that knowledge is first with the view that nazar is first by distinguishing between what must be intended (first in purpose) and what must be performed (first in execution).
This shows the importance of perspective in theological disagreements.
4
Examine Sanusi's preference and reasoning
Sanusi chooses nazar because it is directly urged in the Qur'an and Sunna, unlike its prerequisites which rely on a disputed principle. Also note his refutation of the Isma'ilis and Mu'tazila.
The disputed principle is: 'command to a thing implies command to its necessary means.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبیه على اختلافهم في تعيين أوَّلِ واجب على المُكلَّف. مذهب جماعة منهم الشيخ الأشعري أنَّ أَوَّلَ واجب النظرُ. وذهبَ الأستاذ وإمام الحرمين إلى أنَّ أَوَّلَ واجب : القصد إلى النظر؛ أي : توجيه القلبِ إليه بقطع العلائق المنافية له، ومنها الكِبْرُ والحسد والبغضةُ للعلماءِ الداعينَ إِلى اللهِ سبحانه، وتطهير القلبِ مِنْ هذه الأخلاقِ أَوَّلُ هدايةِ اللهِ للعبدِ. وقال القاضي : أَوَّلُ واجب : أَوَّلُ جزءٍ مِنَ النظرِ. وقيل : أَوَّلُ واجب المعرفةُ، ويُعزى للشيخ أيضاً، وهو في الحقيقة غيرُ مخالف لما قبلَهُ؛ لأنَّهُ نظرَ إلى أَوَّلِ ما يجب مقصداً وغيرَهُ نظرَ إلى أَوَّلِ ما يجب امتثالاً وأداءً. وإنَّما اخترتُ مِنْ هذهِ الأقوالِ القول بأنَّ أَوَّلَ واجب النظرُ لتكرُّرِ الحثّ على النظر في الكتاب والسنة حتى كأنَّهُ مقصد، بخلاف ما قبلَهُ من الوسائل، فإِنَّما أُخِذَ مِنْ قاعدة : ( أَنَّ الأمر بالشيء أمرٌ بما يَتوقَّفُ عليهِ مِنْ فعل المكلَّف )، وفي تلك القاعدة نزاع. ثم هذا النظرُ كافٍ في معرفتِهِ تعالى وإِنْ كانَ بغيرِ مُعلم، خلافاً للإسماعيلية. نعم؛ حصوله بغيرِ مُعلِّم عسير في غاية. وقالَتِ المعتزلة : أَوَّلُ واجب : الشلك[؟]، وهو فاسد.
A note on their disagreement concerning determining the first obligation upon the accountable person. The school of a group, among them al-Shaykh al-Ash'ari, is that the first obligation is nazar (consideration). Al-Ustadh and Imam al-Haramayn held that the first obligation is the intention to engage in nazar, i.e., directing the heart towards it by cutting off attachments that contradict it, including pride, envy, and hatred of the scholars who call to Allah, and purifying the heart from these moral traits is the first guidance of Allah to the servant. Al-Qadi said: The first obligation is the first part of nazar. And it was said: The first obligation is knowledge, and this is also attributed to al-Shaykh, and in reality it does not contradict what came before, because the one looked to the first thing that must be intended, and the other looked to the first thing that must be complied with and performed. And I have chosen from these statements the saying that the first obligation is nazar, because of the repeated urging of nazar in the Book and the Sunna, such that it is like a purpose, unlike what precedes it from among the means, for that is taken from the principle: 'The command to do something is a command to do what is necessary for it from the actions of the accountable person,' and there is dispute over that principle. Moreover, this nazar is sufficient for knowledge of Him, even if without a teacher, contrary to the Isma'ilis. Yes, its attainment without a teacher is extremely difficult. And the Mu'tazila said: The first obligation is doubt (al-shakk[?]), and it is false.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a note (tanbeeh) on the disagreement among theologians about the first obligation upon a morally accountable person (mukallaf). It lists several opinions: 1) The Ash'ari school (attributed to al-Ash'ari himself) says the first obligation is nazar (intellectual consideration). 2) Al-Ustadh (Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini) and Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) hold that the first obligation is the intention (qasd) to engage in nazar, which involves purifying the heart from vices like pride and envy. 3) Al-Qadi (Abu Bakr al-Baqillani) says the first obligation is the first part of nazar itself. 4) Another opinion (attributed to al-Ash'ari as well) says knowledge (ma'rifa) is the first obligation. Sanusi reconciles these by noting that they differ in perspective: one focuses on the first thing intended, the other on the first thing to be performed. Sanusi then states his preference: the first obligation is nazar, because the Qur'an and Sunna repeatedly urge it, making it a primary objective, whereas the prerequisites (like intention) are derived from the principle 'command to a thing implies command to its necessary means'—a principle that is disputed. He adds that nazar suffices for knowledge of God even without a teacher, contrary to the Isma'ilis, though it is extremely difficult without a teacher. Finally, he mentions the Mu'tazila view that the first obligation is doubt (or something similar), which he dismisses as false.

Key Terms
أول واجب
النظر
القصد إلى النظر
المعرفة
قاعدة الأمر بالشيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All the opinions on the first obligation are contradictory and irreconcilable.
Correction: Sanusi shows they can be harmonized by distinguishing between the first thing intended (purpose) and the first thing performed (action).

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi choose the view that the first obligation is nazar itself rather than its prerequisites?
A) Because the Qur'an and Sunna repeatedly emphasize nazar, making it like a goal.
B) Because nazar is easier than intention or knowledge.
C) Because the other views are all false and contradict revelation.
D) Because the principle 'command to a thing implies command to its means' is universally accepted.

Key Takeaway
The first obligation is nazar because it is directly commanded in revelation, while the prerequisites are based on a disputed principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever sincerely engaged in nazar—a deliberate, structured reflection on the existence and nature of your Creator—or do you mostly rely on what you were taught without personal conviction? What would change in your daily life if you made this your first priority?
?
Sanusi mentions that purifying the heart from pride, envy, and hatred of scholars is necessary for directing the heart towards God. Think honestly: which of these attachments still lingers in your heart? Will you take a concrete step this week to uproot it, even if it means humbling yourself?
?
The passage says nazar without a teacher is extremely difficult. Have you sought out a knowledgeable teacher to guide you in understanding the fundamentals of your faith, or have you been satisfied with superficial knowledge? What is holding you back from finding one?

The First Obligation: Different Scholarly Opinions on Intention and Knowledge
Sanusi lists and reconciles early opinions on the first obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various opinions on the first religious obligation and how they are not necessarily contradictory.

Core Idea
The first obligation is debated, but all opinions converge on leading to knowledge of God.

Sanusi begins by surveying several scholarly opinions on what constitutes the first religious duty for a responsible person. He reports views from al-Ash'ari, al-Ustadh, Imam al-Haramayn, al-Qadi, and another attribution to al-Ash'ari, and notes that these views actually converge on the same essential goal. This passage sets the stage for his own preference.

Guided Path
1
Identify each opinion
Read the passage carefully and list the different opinions mentioned. Note who each is attributed to.
There are four distinct opinions in this section.
2
Understand the reconciliation
Notice how Sanusi explains that the view 'first obligation is knowledge' does not contradict the others, by distinguishing intention (maqsad) versus compliance (imtithal).
This shows that apparently conflicting views can be compatible.
3
Reflect on the role of intention
Consider why the intention to investigate (qasd al-naẓar) is emphasized. Why is purifying the heart from pride and envy a prerequisite?
This prepares for Sanusi's own choice in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مذهب جماعة منهم الشيخ الأشعري
وذهب الأستاذ وإمام الحرمين إلى أن أول واجب: القصد إلى النظر؛ أي توجيه القلب إليه بقطع العلائق المنافية له، ومنها الكبر والحسد والبغضة للعلماء الداعين إلى الله سبحانه، وتطهير القلب من هذه الأخلاق أول هداية الله للعبد.
وقال القاضي: أول واجب: أول جزء من النظر.
وقيل: أول واجب المعرفة، ويعزى للشيخ أيضا، وهو في الحقيقة غير مخالف لما قبله؛ لأنه نظر إلى أول ما يجب مقصدا وغيره نظر إلى أول ما يجب امتثالا وأداء.
وإنما اخترت من هذه الأقوال القول بأن أول واجب النظر... لتكرر الحث على النظر في الكتاب والسنة حتى كأنه مقصد، بخلاف ما قبله من الوسائل، فإنما أخذ من قاعدة: أن الأمر بالشيء أمر بما يتوقف عليه من فعل المكلف، وفي تلك القاعدة نزاع.
ثم هذا النظر كاف في معرفته تعالى وإن كان بغير معلم، خلافا للإسماعيلية.
نعم؛ حصوله بغير معلم عسير في غاية.
وقالت المعتزلة: أول واجب: الشك، وهو فاسد؛ أما على أصلنا: فلأن الشك مطلوب بالشرع زواله، فكيف يطلب حصوله؟! وأفي الله شر؟ [إبراهيم:10]، وأما على أصلهم: فلأن الشك كفر، وهو قبيح عندهم لعينه، فلا يكون مأمورا به.
وقيل: أول واجب: الإقرار بالله وبرسوله عن عقد مطابق وإن لم يكن علما، وسيأتي إبطاله عند إبطال القول بصحة التقليد.
هذه أقوال سنة في أول ما يجب، وهي أقرب ما قيل فيه.
The position of a group among them is that of Shaykh al-Ashʿarī. Al-Ustādh and Imām al-Ḥaramayn held that the first obligation is the intention to engage in investigation (al-naẓar); that is, directing the heart towards it by cutting off attachments that are incompatible with it, among which are pride, envy, and hatred of scholars who call to God Almighty, and purifying the heart from these moral vices is the first guidance of God for the servant. Al-Qāḍī said: The first obligation is the first part of investigation. It was said: The first obligation is knowledge (al-maʿrifah), and it is attributed also to the Shaykh. In reality it does not contradict what preceded, because he considered what must be first in terms of intention (maqṣadan), while others considered what must be first in terms of compliance (imtithālan) and performance (adāʾan). I have chosen from these sayings the statement that the first obligation is investigation (al-naẓar)... because of the repeated urging of investigation in the Book and the Sunnah, to the point that it seems like the intended objective, unlike what preceded it among the means, for these are derived from the principle: 'The command for a thing is a command for what it depends on of the responsible person's action,' and there is disagreement regarding that principle. Then this investigation is sufficient for knowing Him, may He be exalted, even if it be without a teacher, contrary to the Ismāʿīliyyah. Yes, obtaining it without a teacher is extremely difficult. The Muʿtazilah said: The first obligation is doubt (al-shakk), and it is invalid. As for our principle: because doubt is something the Law commands its removal, so how can it command its acquisition?! 'Is there any doubt about God?' (Q 14:10). And as for their principle: because doubt is unbelief (kufr), which in their view is evil in itself, so it cannot be commanded. And it was said: The first obligation is affirmation (al-iqrār) of God and His Messenger with a congruent conviction (ʿaqd muṭābiq) even if it is not knowledge, and its refutation will come when refuting the statement regarding the validity of taqlīd. These are six sayings concerning the first thing that is obligatory, and they are the closest that have been said on the matter.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a survey of different scholarly opinions on what constitutes the first religious obligation (awwal wājib) for a responsible person (mukallaf). Sanusi begins with the view of a group including al-Ashʿarī, then reports that al-Ustādh and Imām al-Ḥaramayn hold that the first obligation is the intention (qaṣd) to engage in naẓar (rational inquiry or investigation), which involves directing the heart by removing obstacles like pride and envy. Al-Qāḍī says the first obligation is the very first part of naẓar itself. Another view says it is maʿrifah (knowledge), attributed to al-Ashʿarī, but Sanusi notes this does not really conflict with the others; it focuses on the intended aim whereas the others focus on the first act of compliance. Sanusi then explains why he chooses naẓar: the Qurʾan and Sunnah repeatedly urge it, making it seem like the direct objective, whereas the prior steps are derived from the contested principle that 'commanding a thing commands its prerequisites.' He further notes that such naẓar is sufficient for knowing God even without a teacher, though it is extremely difficult. He refutes the Muʿtazilah position that the first obligation is doubt (shakk), arguing from both the Ashʿarī principle (that the Law aims to remove doubt, not cause it) and the Muʿtazilah's own principle (that doubt is inherently evil and thus cannot be commanded). Finally, he mentions the view that it is iqrār (affirmation) without knowledge, which will be refuted later. The passage closes by noting these are six opinions on the matter.

Key Terms
أول واجب
النظر
القصد إلى النظر
المعرفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, which view about the first obligation is attributed to both al-Ustadh and Imam al-Haramayn?
A) The first obligation is knowledge (ma'rifah).
B) The first obligation is the intention to investigate (qasd al-naẓar).
C) The first obligation is the first part of investigation.
D) The first obligation is doubt (shakk).

Key Takeaway
The various opinions on the first obligation can be reconciled by distinguishing between the intended goal and the first act of compliance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The scholars reconciled their differences by looking at either the goal or the first step. How often do you get stuck in minor disagreements about religious matters while neglecting the purification of your soul? What one practical step can you take today to align your intention with the ultimate goal of knowing God?
?
If your study of this lesson does not lead you to examine your own heart and change a concrete behavior, what is the value of the knowledge? Are you satisfied with merely knowing about these opinions, or will you let them transform you? Pause now and sincerely ask God to show you any hidden vice that contradicts your pursuit of Him.

Why Sanusi Chooses Investigation (Nazar) as the First Obligation
Sanusi defends his choice of nazar and addresses objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's reasoning for selecting nazar as the first obligation and its scriptural basis.

Core Idea
The Qur'an and Sunnah repeatedly urge investigation, making it the clear first duty.

After presenting the various opinions, Sanusi declares his own choice: investigation (al-nazar). He argues that the overwhelming scriptural encouragement for nazar elevates it from a mere means to the intended goal. He also addresses the contested principle that commanding a thing commands its prerequisites, and clarifies that nazar alone suffices for knowing God, though it is extremely difficult.

Guided Path
1
Read Sanusi's declaration
Identify where Sanusi states his choice and the reason: 'because of the repeated urging of investigation in the Book and the Sunnah.'
2
Understand the scriptural argument
Contrast the direct command of nazar with the indirect nature of prerequisites. Why does this make nazar seem like the objective itself?
The principle 'command of a thing is command of its prerequisites' is controversial.
3
Consider the need for a teacher
Sanusi says nazar is sufficient even without a teacher, but it is extremely difficult. What does this imply about the necessity of learning from scholars?
It acknowledges the difficulty but does not negate the obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مذهب جماعة منهم الشيخ الأشعري
وذهب الأستاذ وإمام الحرمين إلى أن أول واجب: القصد إلى النظر؛ أي توجيه القلب إليه بقطع العلائق المنافية له، ومنها الكبر والحسد والبغضة للعلماء الداعين إلى الله سبحانه، وتطهير القلب من هذه الأخلاق أول هداية الله للعبد.
وقال القاضي: أول واجب: أول جزء من النظر.
وقيل: أول واجب المعرفة، ويعزى للشيخ أيضا، وهو في الحقيقة غير مخالف لما قبله؛ لأنه نظر إلى أول ما يجب مقصدا وغيره نظر إلى أول ما يجب امتثالا وأداء.
وإنما اخترت من هذه الأقوال القول بأن أول واجب النظر... لتكرر الحث على النظر في الكتاب والسنة حتى كأنه مقصد، بخلاف ما قبله من الوسائل، فإنما أخذ من قاعدة: أن الأمر بالشيء أمر بما يتوقف عليه من فعل المكلف، وفي تلك القاعدة نزاع.
ثم هذا النظر كاف في معرفته تعالى وإن كان بغير معلم، خلافا للإسماعيلية.
نعم؛ حصوله بغير معلم عسير في غاية.
وقالت المعتزلة: أول واجب: الشك، وهو فاسد؛ أما على أصلنا: فلأن الشك مطلوب بالشرع زواله، فكيف يطلب حصوله؟! وأفي الله شر؟ [إبراهيم:10]، وأما على أصلهم: فلأن الشك كفر، وهو قبيح عندهم لعينه، فلا يكون مأمورا به.
وقيل: أول واجب: الإقرار بالله وبرسوله عن عقد مطابق وإن لم يكن علما، وسيأتي إبطاله عند إبطال القول بصحة التقليد.
هذه أقوال سنة في أول ما يجب، وهي أقرب ما قيل فيه.
The position of a group among them is that of Shaykh al-Ashʿarī. Al-Ustādh and Imām al-Ḥaramayn held that the first obligation is the intention to engage in investigation (al-naẓar); that is, directing the heart towards it by cutting off attachments that are incompatible with it, among which are pride, envy, and hatred of scholars who call to God Almighty, and purifying the heart from these moral vices is the first guidance of God for the servant. Al-Qāḍī said: The first obligation is the first part of investigation. It was said: The first obligation is knowledge (al-maʿrifah), and it is attributed also to the Shaykh. In reality it does not contradict what preceded, because he considered what must be first in terms of intention (maqṣadan), while others considered what must be first in terms of compliance (imtithālan) and performance (adāʾan). I have chosen from these sayings the statement that the first obligation is investigation (al-naẓar)... because of the repeated urging of investigation in the Book and the Sunnah, to the point that it seems like the intended objective, unlike what preceded it among the means, for these are derived from the principle: 'The command for a thing is a command for what it depends on of the responsible person's action,' and there is disagreement regarding that principle. Then this investigation is sufficient for knowing Him, may He be exalted, even if it be without a teacher, contrary to the Ismāʿīliyyah. Yes, obtaining it without a teacher is extremely difficult. The Muʿtazilah said: The first obligation is doubt (al-shakk), and it is invalid. As for our principle: because doubt is something the Law commands its removal, so how can it command its acquisition?! 'Is there any doubt about God?' (Q 14:10). And as for their principle: because doubt is unbelief (kufr), which in their view is evil in itself, so it cannot be commanded. And it was said: The first obligation is affirmation (al-iqrār) of God and His Messenger with a congruent conviction (ʿaqd muṭābiq) even if it is not knowledge, and its refutation will come when refuting the statement regarding the validity of taqlīd. These are six sayings concerning the first thing that is obligatory, and they are the closest that have been said on the matter.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a survey of different scholarly opinions on what constitutes the first religious obligation (awwal wājib) for a responsible person (mukallaf). Sanusi begins with the view of a group including al-Ashʿarī, then reports that al-Ustādh and Imām al-Ḥaramayn hold that the first obligation is the intention (qaṣd) to engage in naẓar (rational inquiry or investigation), which involves directing the heart by removing obstacles like pride and envy. Al-Qāḍī says the first obligation is the very first part of naẓar itself. Another view says it is maʿrifah (knowledge), attributed to al-Ashʿarī, but Sanusi notes this does not really conflict with the others; it focuses on the intended aim whereas the others focus on the first act of compliance. Sanusi then explains why he chooses naẓar: the Qurʾan and Sunnah repeatedly urge it, making it seem like the direct objective, whereas the prior steps are derived from the contested principle that 'commanding a thing commands its prerequisites.' He further notes that such naẓar is sufficient for knowing God even without a teacher, though it is extremely difficult. He refutes the Muʿtazilah position that the first obligation is doubt (shakk), arguing from both the Ashʿarī principle (that the Law aims to remove doubt, not cause it) and the Muʿtazilah's own principle (that doubt is inherently evil and thus cannot be commanded). Finally, he mentions the view that it is iqrār (affirmation) without knowledge, which will be refuted later. The passage closes by noting these are six opinions on the matter.

Key Terms
النظر
الكتاب والسنة
الأمر بالشيء أمر بما يتوقف عليه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is Sanusi's main scriptural reason for choosing nazar as the first obligation?
A) The Qur'an and Sunnah repeatedly urge investigation.
B) All scholars agree on it.
C) It is the easiest action.
D) It does not require any prerequisites.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's choice of nazar is based on direct scriptural commands, making it the clear first duty despite practical difficulties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever relied solely on a teacher or scholar without personally investigating the evidence from the Qur'an and Sunnah? What would it cost you to start taking that responsibility yourself?
?
Sanusi says nazar is sufficient but extremely difficult without a teacher. Are you using the difficulty as an excuse to avoid the hard work of learning your religion directly from the sources?
?
If the first obligation is indeed investigation, what are you postponing by not engaging in it today? What step can you take right now to fulfill this duty?

Refuting the Mu'tazila View: Why Doubt Cannot Be the First Obligation
Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazila view of doubt and mentions the affirmation view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's refutation of the Mu'tazila claim that doubt is the first obligation, using both Ash'ari and Mu'tazila principles.

Core Idea
Doubt cannot be commanded because the Law aims to remove it, and it is inherently evil.

Sanusi now turns to refute a radically different opinion: the Mu'tazila claim that the first obligation is doubt (shakk). He offers two refutations: from the Ash'ari principle that the Law commands removal of doubt, and from the Mu'tazila's own principle that doubt is evil in itself. He also briefly notes the affirmation view and defers its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazila position
Find where Sanusi reports that the Mu'tazila said the first obligation is doubt.
This is a radical view that goes against common sense.
2
First refutation: Ash'ari principle
Sanusi argues: The Law commands removing doubt (e.g., 'Is there any doubt about God?'), so it cannot command acquiring it.
This relies on the purpose of revelation.
3
Second refutation: Mu'tazila's own principle
Even according to the Mu'tazila, doubt is kufr, which is evil in itself, so it cannot be commanded.
This uses their own ethical framework against them.
4
Note the affirmation view
Sanusi mentions the view that the first obligation is affirmation (iqrār) without knowledge, but says its refutation will come later.
This is a preview of a later discussion on taqlid.

The Six Certain Premises of Demonstration
Sanusi lists and defines the six types of certain premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six types of certain premises that form the basis of demonstration (burhān) in Islamic logic and how each type provides certainty.

Core Idea
Demonstration rests on six types of certain premises, each with its own source of certainty.

Sanusi introduces the building blocks of demonstrative reasoning by classifying the types of certain premises that can serve as starting points for proof. Without understanding these categories, one cannot assess the strength of a logical argument. This passage defines each type with clear examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the flow. Note that the first type is given only as examples without a label. Then read the English translation.
The first type is traditionally called 'badīhiyyāt' (self-evident).
2
Identify each of the six types
Go through the passage and list the six types in order. For each, note its definition and the example provided. Pay attention to the distinctions between experiential and intuitive.
The sixth type (mutawātirāt) is said to combine the second and third types.
3
Understand the purpose of the classification
The passage ends by stating that these six types together compose demonstration, whose goal is certain knowledge. Consider why each type is considered 'certain' in its own way.
Demonstration is the highest form of proof in logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الواحد نصفُ الاثنين ، والكل أعظمُ مِنْ جزئه . - و ومشاهدات : وتُسمَّى أيضاً : حِسَّيَّاتٍ ؛ وهي ما يجزمُ بهِ العقل بواسطة حسّ ؛ كقولنا : الشمس مشرقة ، والنار محرقة. - وقضايا قياساتها معها : وهي ما يجزمُ بهِ العقلُ بوسط يَتصوَّرُهُ معها (۱) ؛ كقولنا : الأربعةُ زوج ؛ فإنَّه بسبب وسط حاضر في الذهن ؛ وهو الانقسام بمتساويين (۲) وتجريبيَّات : وهي ما يجزمُ بهِ العقل بواسطة ترتبه مراراً كثيرة (۳) ؛ بحيثُ يجزمُ العقلُ بأنَّهُ ليسَ على سبيل الاتفاق ؛ كقولنا : السَّقَمُونِيا تُسهل الصفراء - وحدسيات : وهي ما يجزم به العقل لترتُبِ دون ترتُبِ التجريبيَّاتِ معَ مصاحبةِ القرائنِ ؛ كقولنا : نور القمرِ مستفاد مِنْ نورِ الشمس ومتواتراتٌ : وهي ما يجزمُ بهِ العقلُ بواسطة حِسّ السمع ووسط وو حاضر في الذهنِ ؛ وذلكَ أَنْ يُخبِرَ عن محسوس يمكنُ وقوعه جمع كثير يجزم العقلُ بامتناع تواطئهم على الكذب ؛ كقولنا : محمد رسولُ اللهِ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ ادَّعى النبوَّةَ ، وظهرَتِ المعجزات على يديه ، وهذا القسمُ مُركَّبٌ مِنَ القسم الثاني والثالث فهذه الأقسام السنةُ منها يتركَّبُ البرهانُ . والغرض منه : حصول العلم اليقيني .
The one is half of two, and the whole is greater than its part. And observations (mushāhadāt): also called sensibles (ḥissiyyāt); they are what the mind judges by means of sense; like our statement: the sun is shining, and fire is burning. And propositions with their syllogisms (qaḍāyā qiyāsātuhā maʿahā): they are what the mind judges by a middle term it conceives with them; like our statement: four is even; for it is due to a middle term present in the mind, which is division into two equal halves. And experiential (tajribiyyāt): they are what the mind judges by repeated occurrence many times, such that the mind judges that it is not by coincidence; like our statement: scammony induces bile. And intuitive (ḥadsiyyāt): they are what the mind judges due to an occurrence less than that of experiential, with the accompaniment of indications; like our statement: the light of the moon is derived from the light of the sun. And mutawātirāt (mutawātirāt): they are what the mind judges by means of the sense of hearing and a middle term present in the mind; that is, when a numerous group informs about something perceptible that is possible to occur, and the mind judges that it is impossible for them to conspire on lying; like our statement: Muhammad, the Messenger of God (peace be upon him), claimed prophethood, and miracles appeared at his hands. And this type is composed of the second and third types. So these six types — from them demonstration (burhān) is composed. And its purpose is: the attainment of certain knowledge (al-ʿilm al-yaqīnī).
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the six types of certain premises that together constitute demonstration (burhān). The author begins with an example of the first type (بديهيات, though not named here): 'The one is half of two, and the whole is greater than its part' — these are self-evident truths. Then he defines the second type: observations (mushāhadāt), also called sensibles (ḥissiyyāt), which are judgments made by the mind through the senses, such as 'the sun is shining' or 'fire is burning'. The third type: propositions with their syllogisms (qaḍāyā qiyāsātuhā maʿahā) — judgments made by a middle term present in the mind, e.g., 'four is even' because it is divisible into two equal parts. The fourth type: experiential (tajribiyyāt), where repeated observation leads the mind to conclude a causal connection as not coincidental, e.g., 'scammony induces bile'. The fifth type: intuitive (ḥadsiyyāt), similar to experiential but with fewer repetitions and accompanied by signs, e.g., 'the light of the moon is derived from the sun'. The sixth type: mutawātirāt — reports from a large group that preclude collusion, e.g., the prophethood of Muhammad and his miracles; this type combines elements of the second and third types. The passage concludes that these six types are the components of demonstration, whose purpose is certain knowledge.

Key Terms
بديهيات (unlabeled)
مشاهدات
حسيات
قضايا قياساتها معها
تجربيات
حدسيات
متواترات
برهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some students think these six types are arbitrary or not exhaustive in all contexts of reasoning.
Correction: These are the standard categories of certain premises in Islamic logic as used in kalam, derived from Aristotle's Posterior Analytics, and are considered exhaustive for premises that yield certainty in demonstrative proofs.

Checkpoint
Which type of premise is defined as 'what the mind judges through repeated experience such that it knows it is not coincidental'?
A) مشاهدات
B) تجربيات
C) حدسيات
D) متواترات

Key Takeaway
The six types of certain premises — self-evident, observational, syllogistic, experiential, intuitive, and mutawātir — are the foundation of demonstrative reasoning, each providing certainty in its own way.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You say you believe in Islam with certainty. Which of the six types of certain premises does your 'certainty' actually fall into? Is it rooted in mutawātir reports, or in something less rigorous? Are you comfortable with that foundation?
?
The passage mentions that mutawātir reports are certain because it is impossible for a large group to conspire on a lie. Have you ever doubted a well-known Islamic report (e.g., the splitting of the moon) and rationalized it away? What does that tell you about your commitment to the sources of certain knowledge?
?
When you proclaim 'I know there is a God,' which premise type are you relying on? Is it intuitive, experiential, or something else? If it's not built on solid burhān, what are you willing to change so that your knowledge becomes certain?

Dialectic (Jadal): Definition and Purpose
Sanusi defines dialectic (jadal) and its purpose

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of dialectic (jadal) as a mode of argument based on well-known premises and its twofold purpose of persuasion or refutation.

Core Idea
Dialectic uses widely accepted premises to persuade or refute.

Sanusi now introduces a distinct method of reasoning: dialectic (jadal). Unlike demonstration, which relies on certain premises, dialectic operates with 'well-known premises'—claims accepted by the public for practical or customary reasons. This passage defines dialectic, illustrates it with concrete examples, and explains its purpose in theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text of the passage once, then follow along with the translation. Notice the clarity of the definition and the examples.
The examples are typical moral judgments that people accept.
2
Understand the definition of jadal
Identify that jadal is composed of muqaddimāt mashhūra (well-known premises). Note the characterization of these premises as accepted by the public for a general interest or due to custom.
Well-known premises are not certain but widely acknowledged.
3
Examine the examples
Look at each example: oppression, exposing private parts, consoling the poor, avenging a brother's murder. See how each uses a general premise (e.g., 'every oppression is ugly') to reach a conclusion.
These examples rely on common moral intuitions.
4
Identify the two purposes
Notice the twofold purpose: to persuade someone who cannot grasp demonstration, or to compel and refute an opponent in debate.
Dialectic is thus a practical tool for argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجدلُ : فهو ما تأَلَّفَ مِنْ مُقدِّمات مشهورة ، والمُقدِّماتُ المشهورةُ : ما اعترف به الجمهور لمصلحة عامة أو لسبب رفة أو حميَّة ؛ كقولنا : هذا ظلم ، وكلُّ ظلم قبيح ؛ فهذا قبيح وهذا كاشف العورتِهِ ، وكلُّ كاشف لعورته فهو مذموم ؛ فهذا مذموم . وهذا فقير ، وكلُّ فقير تُحمَدُ مواساته ؛ فهذا تُحمَدُ مواساته وهذا قُتِلَ أخوه ، وكلُّ مَنْ قُتِلَ أخوهُ حَسُنَ أَنْ يقتل قاتلَهُ ؛ فهذا حسُنَ أن يقتل قاتله . والغرضُ مِنَ الجدلِ : إمَّا إقناع قاصر عن البرهان ، أو إلزام الخصم ودفعه .
And as for dialectic (jadal): it is that which is composed of well-known premises (muqaddimāt mashhūra). And well-known premises are what the public acknowledges for a general interest or for a reason of [؟] or zeal; like our statement: this is oppression, and every oppression is ugly; therefore this is ugly. And this is one who exposes his private parts, and everyone who exposes his private parts is blameworthy; therefore this is blameworthy. And this is poor, and every poor person is praised for consoling him; therefore consoling him is praised. And this one's brother was killed, and for everyone whose brother was killed it is good that he kills his killer; therefore it is good that he kills his killer. And the purpose of dialectic is: either to persuade one incapable of demonstration, or to compel and refute the opponent.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage defines dialectic (jadal) as a type of argument composed of well-known premises (muqaddimāt mashhūra). Well-known premises are those accepted by the public for a general benefit or due to a customary motive like modesty or zeal. The author provides several examples: 'This is oppression, and every oppression is ugly; therefore this is ugly' — this relies on a commonly accepted moral judgment. Another example: exposing one's private parts is blameworthy. Another: a poor person should be consoled. Another: a brother's killer may be killed. These are not certain but are widely accepted. The purpose of dialectic is twofold: to persuade someone who cannot grasp demonstration, or to compel and refute an opponent (i.e., in debate). Thus dialectic is a tool for argumentation, not for attaining certain knowledge.

Key Terms
جدل
مقدمات مشهورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dialectic is always inferior to demonstration and has no legitimate use.
Correction: Dialectic serves a valid purpose in persuading those who cannot grasp demonstration and in refuting opponents in debate.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of dialectic (jadal) according to Sanusi?
A) To attain certain knowledge
B) To persuade the incapable of demonstration or compel the opponent
C) To motivate the audience
D) To produce new scientific truths

Key Takeaway
Dialectic (jadal) is argumentation using widely accepted premises to persuade or compel an opponent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Rhetoric: Its Premises and Purpose
Sanusi defines rhetoric and its purpose

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of rhetoric in Islamic logic, its two types of premises (accepted and probable), and its purpose of motivating listeners.

Core Idea
Rhetoric seeks probable assent, not certain knowledge.

In the previous lessons, Sanusi covered demonstrative and dialectical arguments. Now he turns to rhetoric (khataba), a type of argument that aims not at certainty but at persuasion. This passage lays out its two kinds of premises—accepted and probable—and explains that its ultimate goal is to move the audience to action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: definition of rhetoric, then two types of premises with an example, then the purpose.
Pay attention to the key terms 'maqbūla' and 'maẓnūna'.
2
Identify the two types of premises
The passage distinguishes premises accepted from a trustworthy person and probable premises. Find the example for probable premises and see how it works.
3
Understand the purpose of rhetoric
Sanusi explicitly states the purpose: to motivate listeners (targhīb al-sāmiʿīn). Reflect on how this differs from demonstration, which aims at certainty.
This purpose explains why rhetoric is used in sermons and political speeches.
4
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to check if you grasped the main point of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الخطابةُ : فهي ما تأَلَّفَ مِنْ مُقدِّماتٍ مقبولةٍ مِنْ شخص معتقد فيهِ الصدقُ لسرّ لا يُطَّلع عليه ، أو لصفة جميلة ؛ كزيادة علم أو زهد ونحوه ، أو مِنْ مُقدِّماتٍ مظنونةٍ ؛ مثلُ : هذا يدور في الليل بالسلاح ، وكلُّ مَنْ يدورُ في الليل بالسلاح فهو لص ؛ فهذا لص . والغرضُ مِنَ الخطابة : ترغيب السامعين
And as for rhetoric (khaṭāba): it is that which is composed of premises accepted (muqaddimāt maqbūla) from a person believed to be truthful for a secret not disclosed, or for a beautiful quality; like extra knowledge or asceticism and the like; or from probable premises (muqaddimāt maẓnūna); such as: this one walks at night with a weapon, and everyone who walks at night with a weapon is a thief; therefore this is a thief. And the purpose of rhetoric is: to motivate the listeners.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage defines rhetoric (khaṭāba) as an argument composed of two types of premises: first, accepted premises (muqaddimāt maqbūla) taken from a person believed to be truthful, either for a hidden reason or for a virtuous quality (e.g., knowledge or asceticism); second, probable premises (muqaddimāt maẓnūna), which are based on likelihood rather than certainty. An example of the latter: someone wandering at night with a weapon is likely a thief. The purpose of rhetoric is not to produce certainty but to motivate listeners (targhīb al-sāmiʿīn) — that is, to influence their will or emotions toward a desired action.

Key Terms
خطابة
مقدمات مقبولة
مقدمات مظنونة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rhetoric is just about eloquent speech or stylistic beauty.
Correction: In Islamic logic, rhetoric is a specific type of argument using accepted or probable premises, not merely decorative language.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of rhetoric (khataba) according to Sanusi?
A) To establish certain knowledge
B) To refute an opponent
C) To motivate the listeners
D) To provide self-evident truths

Key Takeaway
Rhetoric is an argument built from accepted or probable premises, intended to motivate listeners rather than produce certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Three Types of Rational Proofs in Kalam
Sanusi enumerates three divisions of rational proofs and explains that only the demonstrative proof is relied upon.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three divisions of rational proofs in kalam and why only the demonstrative proof (burhan) composed of certain premises is considered authoritative for establishing religious beliefs.

Core Idea
Not every argument counts – only demonstrative proof leads to certainty.

In this passage, Sanusi categorizes rational proofs based on the nature of their premises. He distinguishes three types: demonstrative, rhetorical, and fallacious. He then clarifies that for theological matters, only the first type, the demonstrative proof composed of certain premises, is reliable. This lesson will walk you through these categories and the reasoning behind their exclusivity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three divisions
Read the Arabic passage and locate the three names Sanusi gives for rational proofs: burhan, khitaba/amara, and mughalata.
These correspond to demonstrative, rhetorical, and fallacious proofs.
2
Understand the basis of division
Notice that the division is based on the type of premises: certain (qat'iyya), probable (zanniyya), or resembling either.
This is key to evaluating the strength of an argument.
3
Note the primacy of burhan
Sanusi states that only the first division (burhan) is relied upon for correcting religious beliefs. Understand why certainty is required in matters of faith.
4
Reflect on the technical terms
Sanusi explains his use of 'qati'a' and 'sutu' to emphasize certainty. Consider how these terms reinforce the need for conclusive proof in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذه أقسام الحجة العقلية وجعلها البيضاوي في الطوالع ثلاثة أقسام: البرهان، والخطابة وتسمى أيضاً الأمارة، والمغالطة؛ لأن الحجة العقلية إما أن تتركب من مقدمات قطعية أو من مقدمات ظنية أو من شبيهة بأحداهما، وتسمى الأولى برهاناً ودليلاً، والثانية خطابة وأمارة، والثالثة مغالطة. وبالجملة فالمعتمد من هذه الأقسام في تصحيح العقائد الدينية القسم الأول الذي هو البرهان، فلذا قلت: من البراهين ووصفتها بالقاطعة لكشف معناها، وإنما عطفت عليها الأدلة عطف عام على خاص؛ لتدخل في ذلك الأدلة العقلية فيما تقبل فيه من العقائد؛ وذلك كل ما لا تتوقف المعجزة عليه كنفي النقائص عنه تعالى وثبوت الوحدانية له على رأي وكوقوع بعض الممكنات من الحشر والرؤية ونحوهما، ووصفتها بالسطوع إشارة إلى اشتراط القطع فيها أيضاً، ولو كان بدل هذا الكلام يقال: من البراهين العقلية والقواطع السمعية لكان أبين وأحسن أنَّ
So these are the divisions of the rational proof. Al-Baydawi made them in al-Tawali' three divisions: the demonstrative proof (burhan), the rhetorical proof (al-khitaba) which is also called al-amara (indication), and the fallacious proof (al-mughalata); because the rational proof is either composed of certain premises, or of probable premises, or of something resembling one of the two. The first is called burhan and dalil, the second khitaba and amara, and the third mughalata. In summary, the relied-upon among these divisions for correcting religious beliefs is the first division, which is the burhan. Hence I said: 'from burhans' and described them as 'conclusive' (al-qati'a) to reveal its meaning. And I adjoined to them 'al-adilla' (proofs) as a general to a specific, so that rational proofs enter in what they are accepted among beliefs. And that is everything on which a miracle does not depend, such as negating imperfections from Him Most High, establishing unicity for Him according to an opinion, and the occurrence of some possible things like the resurrection, vision, and the like. And I described them as 'bright' (al-sutu') to indicate the condition of certainty in them as well. And if instead of this statement it were said: 'from rational burhans and scriptural al-qawati' (conclusives), it would be clearer and better, that...
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage defines the divisions of rational proofs and clarifies which division is authoritative in theology. The author states that rational proofs are divided into three types based on their premises: 1) demonstrative proof (burhan) composed of certain premises, which is also called dalil; 2) rhetorical proof or indication (khitaba/amara) composed of probable premises; and 3) fallacious proof (mughalata) resembling one of the two. Then the author emphasizes that only the first type, the burhan, is relied upon for correcting religious beliefs. He explains why he used the term 'burhans' and described them as 'conclusive' – to indicate that only certain proofs are acceptable. He also notes that he added 'adilla' (proofs) as a general term to include rational proofs in those matters where they are accepted, namely matters that do not depend on a miracle, such as negating imperfection from God and establishing His unicity, as well as some possible things like resurrection and vision. He further explains that describing them as 'bright' (sutu') implies the requirement of certainty. Finally, he suggests that if instead one said 'from rational burhans and scriptural conclusives', it would be clearer.

Correction: Only demonstrative proof (burhan) composed of certain premises is relied upon for establishing religious beliefs; probable or fallacious arguments are not sufficient.

A gloss can restrict what was previously stated absolutely.

In this short passage, Sanusi clarifies that a phrase in his own text serves to restrict a statement that was left absolute in the well-known work 'al-Irshad' and other sources. This demonstrates the careful precision of theological writing, where later qualifications refine earlier general claims.

Obligation of Knowledge: General vs. Detailed Proof
Sanusi discusses obligation levels for doctrinal knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between individual and communal obligations regarding theological knowledge and the roles of general and detailed proofs.

Core Idea
Not everyone must be a theologian; a general proof suffices for every believer.

Sanusi clarifies an important point about who is obligated to know the details of theology. He distinguishes between what every individual must know (general proof) and what only some need to master (detailed proof). This passage resolves a common confusion about the duty of acquiring knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the original Arabic passage and the literal English translation. Focus on the key terms: fard 'ayn, fard kifaya, mandub.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the three positions
Notice that Sanusi presents views from three scholars: al-'Amidi (quoting al-Imam), al-Fahri, and Ibn Rushd. Each gives a different ruling on detailed proof.
3
Reflect on the implications
Think about what this means for the average believer. They must have a general proof (fard 'ayn), but detailed proof is only required of the community as a whole (fard kifaya) or recommended (mandub).
This shows the importance of scholarship while not overburdening everyone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي وجوب المعرفةِ على الأعيانِ بالدليل الإجمالي وعلى الكفاية بالتفصيلي .. نقلا الآمدي عن الإمام وغيره قائلاً : من كان اعتقاده دون دليل ولا شبهة .. فهو مؤمنٌ عاص بترك النظرِ . الفهري : لا نزاع بين المتكلمين في عدم وجوب المعرفة بالدليل التفصيلي على الأعيانِ ، وإنما هو كفاية ، وظاهر قول ابن رشد في « نوازله » : إنما هي بالدليل التفصيلي .. مندوب إليه ، لا فرض كفاية . انتهى .
And regarding the obligation of knowledge upon individuals by a general proof and communally by a detailed proof... Al-‘Amidi transmitted from al-Imam and others saying: 'Whoever's belief is without proof and without doubt... is a believer sinful for abandoning reflection.' Al-Fahri: There is no dispute among the mutakallimun that knowledge by a detailed proof is not individually obligatory, but rather communally obligatory. And the apparent meaning of Ibn Rushd's statement in his Nawazil: It is by detailed proof... recommended, not a communal obligation. End.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage addresses the obligation of knowing theological truths. It distinguishes between two types of evidence: general (ijmali) and detailed (tafsili). According to al-‘Amidi and al-Imam, the one who holds belief without any evidence or even a doubt is a believer but sinful for abandoning reflection. Al-Fahri asserts that there is consensus among mutakallimun that detailed proof is not required of each individual; it is a communal obligation (fard kifaya). Ibn Rushd, in his Nawazil, goes further, stating that detailed proof is merely recommended (mandub) and not even a communal obligation.

Key Terms
fard 'ayn
fard kifaya
dalil ijmalī
dalil tafsīlī
mandub

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that every individual must be a mutakallim (theologian) capable of detailed proofs.
Correction: This passage shows that only a general understanding is individually obligatory, while detailed proofs are for the scholars (communal obligation or even merely recommended).

Checkpoint
According to al-Fahri, knowledge through detailed proof is:
A) Individually obligatory (fard 'ayn).
B) Communally obligatory (fard kifaya).
C) Recommended (mandub).
D) Forbidden.

Key Takeaway
The obligation of theological knowledge varies: general proof is individually obligatory, while detailed proof is communal or recommended.

The Obligation of Reflective Knowledge
The passage presents rulings on the one who abandons reflection and the obligation of knowledge by general and detailed evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the obligation of acquiring knowledge of creed through reflection, and the differing rulings on one who dies without reflecting.

Core Idea
Reflective knowledge of creed is individually obligatory; abandoning it incurs sin, and possibly disbelief.

The passage opens with a critical scenario: a person who has time to reflect but chooses not to, and dies in that state. The author presents two positions from al-Qadi on whether such a person is a disbeliever. Then the discussion moves to the types of evidence required for knowledge and their obligations.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening scenario
Read the Arabic passage from 'اختياراً .. كافر' to the end of the first sentence. Notice the two sayings of al-Qadi.
The ellipsis indicates a missing part; focus on the rulings.
2
Identify the two types of evidence
The passage distinguishes between general evidence (dalīl ijmālī) and detailed evidence (dalīl tafsīlī). Understand the difference.
General evidence is an overall proof; detailed evidence is a full logical demonstration.
3
Understand the obligation levels
Knowledge by general evidence is individually obligatory; knowledge by detailed evidence is a collective obligation. The quote from al-Āmidī clarifies that one who holds belief without evidence or doubt is a believer but sinful.
This shows that some reflection is obligatory, but not necessarily detailed proof.

وفي وجوب المعرفةِ على الأعيانِ بالدليل الإجمالي وعلى الكفاية بالتفصيلي .. نقلا الآمدي عن الإمام وغيرِهِ قائلاً : « مَنْ كَانَ اعتقادُهُ دون دليل ولا شبهة .. فهو مؤمنٌ عاص بترك النظرِ »
الفهري (۲) : لا نزاع بين المتكلمين في عدم وجوب المعرفة بالدليل التفصيلي على الأعيانِ ، وإنَّما هو كفاية ، وظاهر قول ابن رشد في « نوازلِهِ » : إنَّما هي بالدليل التفصيلي .. مندوب إليه ، لا فرضُ كفاية ( انتهى)
قلت : وبالجملة : فالذي حكاه غيرُ واحدٍ عن جمهور أهل السنَّةِ ومحققيهم : أنَّ التقليد لا يكفي في العقائدِ ، ولهذا قال ابنُ الحاجبِ في العقيدة المنسوبة لهُ بعدَ قولِهِ : ( إِنَّ الإيمان هو التصديق ، وهو حديث النفس التابع للمعرفة، لا المعرفة على الأصح ) ، قال : ( ولا يكفي التقليد في ذلكَ على الأصح ( انتهى
...by choice? disbeliever, and if he dies before the passing of time that allows that while leaving off reflection by choice in what he comprehended from it... the two sayings of al-Qadi: 'the correct opinion is his disbelief,' after his statement: 'it is possible that he not be declared a disbeliever.' And concerning the obligation of knowledge upon individuals by general evidence and collective obligation by detailed evidence... quoting al-Āmidī from al-Imām and others saying: 'Whoever's belief is without evidence or doubt... he is a believer disobedient by leaving reflection.' Al-Fahrī: 'There is no disagreement among the theologians concerning the non-obligation of knowledge by detailed evidence upon individuals, rather it is collective obligation, and the apparent statement of Ibn Rushd in his Nawāzil: it is by detailed evidence... recommended, not a collective obligation.' (end) I say: In summary, what more than one have narrated from the majority of Ahl al-Sunnah and their verifiers is that taqlīd is not sufficient in matters of creed. For this reason Ibn al-Ḥājib said in the creed attributed to him after his statement: 'Indeed faith is assenting, and it is inner speech following knowledge, not knowledge itself according to the correct view.' He said: 'And taqlīd is not sufficient in that according to the correct view.' (end)
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage discusses the obligation of knowledge (maʿrifa) in matters of creed and addresses the role of reflection (nazar) and the sufficiency of taqlīd. It begins with a scenario: someone who dies after having had sufficient time to reflect but chooses to abandon reflection. The author presents two sayings of al-Qadi: the correct view is that such a person is a disbeliever, but there is also a view that he might not be declared a disbeliever. This shows the disagreement. Then it moves to the obligation of knowledge. It states that knowledge by general evidence (dalīl ijmālī) is obligatory upon every individual, while knowledge by detailed evidence (dalīl tafsīlī) is a collective obligation. Al-Āmidī is quoted saying that one who holds a belief without any evidence or even a doubt is still a believer but is sinful for abandoning reflection. This indicates that some level of reasoning is obligatory. Al-Fahrī then clarifies that there is no disagreement among theologians that detailed evidence is not individually obligatory; it is only a collective obligation. He also notes the apparent position of Ibn Rushd that detailed evidence is recommended, not obligatory. The author then summarizes the position of the majority of Ahl al-Sunnah and their verifiers: that taqlīd is not sufficient in matters of creed. He supports this by quoting Ibn al-Ḥājib, who defines faith as assent (taṣdīq) which is inner speech following knowledge, not knowledge itself. Ibn al-Ḥājib explicitly states that taqlīd is not sufficient. Thus the passage establishes the consensus that reflective knowledge is required for valid faith, and taqlīd alone does not suffice.

معرفة
دليل إجمالي
دليل تفصيلي

Reflective knowledge of creed is individually obligatory, and abandoning reflection renders one sinful, with some scholars ruling it as disbelief.

The Insufficiency of Taqlid in Creed
The passage clarifies the obligation levels of detailed evidence and states the consensus that taqlid is insufficient.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that taqlid (blind imitation) is not sufficient for correct faith, according to the majority of Ahl al-Sunnah.

Core Idea
Taqlid alone cannot ground valid belief; reflective knowledge is necessary.

After establishing the obligation of reflection, the passage addresses the question of taqlid. Al-Fahrī reports consensus that detailed evidence is not individually obligatory, and Ibn Rushd considers it recommended. The author then summarizes the position of the majority: taqlid is insufficient in creed, and quotes Ibn al-Ḥājib to affirm that faith requires knowledge, not mere imitation.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Fahrī's clarification
Read from 'الفهري (۲) : لا نزاع...' to the end. Note that there is agreement that detailed evidence is a collective obligation, not individual.
This shows that not everyone must master detailed proofs, but some must provide them.
2
Note Ibn Rushd's view
Ibn Rushd holds that detailed evidence is recommended (mandūb), not even a collective obligation. This is a minority view.
3
Understand the consensus on taqlid
The author declares that the majority of Ahl al-Sunnah hold that taqlid is not sufficient. Ibn al-Ḥājib's definition of faith as 'inner speech following knowledge' supports this, as taqlid lacks true knowledge.
Taqlid means accepting a belief without evidence or reasoning.

Refuting the Objection from the Prophet's Practice and Clarifying the Ruling on Teaching
Sanusi refutes the claim that the Prophet's acceptance of apparent faith justifies taqlid and explains the limits of not questioning beliefs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Prophet's acceptance of outward faith does not justify taqlid for salvation, and the conditional obligation to teach when manifest error appears.

Core Idea
Outward acceptance is for this world; true faith is required for the hereafter.

Sanusi addresses a common objection: if the Prophet and Companions accepted people's mere verbal confession without investigating their inner faith, doesn't that make taqlid acceptable? He responds by distinguishing between worldly judgments based on outward appearances and what actually saves a person in the afterlife. Then he qualifies al-Ghazali's statement about not disturbing common beliefs, explaining that teaching becomes obligatory when manifest error appears.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the flow of the argument. Notice the objection, the response, and the final qualification.
Pay attention to the author's own phrase 'فإنها غير مخلصة في الآخرة'.
2
Identify the key distinction
Sanusi distinguishes between worldly rulings (أحكام الدنيا) which are based on outward appearances (الظواهر) and what saves in the afterlife (الآخرة). Understand why this distinction is crucial to refuting the objection.
The example of hypocrites illustrates this distinction clearly.
3
Examine al-Ghazali's statement and its condition
Find the quotation from al-Ghazali: 'لا تحرك عقائد العامة ويتركون على ما هم عليه'. Then see how Sanusi adds a condition: 'ما لم يظهر المنكر في عقائدهم' — when manifest error appears, teaching becomes obligatory.
This shows that the principle is not absolute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما اغترَ بهِ القائلُ بصحَةِ التقليدِ ؛ مِن اكتفاء رسولِ اللهِ صلَّى اللهُ عليه وسلم وأصحابه رضي الله عنهم بإجراء أحكام الإسلام ورفع وسلَّمَ القتالِ بمجرَّدِ النطق بكلمتي الإيمانِ مِنْ غيرِ بحث منهـم علـى السرائر . . فلا دليل فيهِ ؛ لأنَّ ذلكَ إنَّما هو مِنْ بابِ إجراء الأحكام على المظان والظواهر ، وليس كلامنا فيهِ ، وإِنَّما كلامنا فيما بينَ العبدِ وربَّهِ ، وفيما ينجيهِ مِنَ الخلودِ معَ سائرِ الكفرة في النار ، وقد أجرى النبيُّ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ أحكام الإسلام على مَنْ قُطِعَ بأردا كفرٍ فِيهِ مِنَ المنافقين ، ولم يدلّ ذلك على أنَّهم كذلك في الآخرة ، وإلى هذا المعنى أشرتُ بقولي : ( فإنَّها غيرُ مُخلَّصةٍ في الآخرةِ ) ؛ أي : وأما في الدنيا : فمبنى أحكامها على الظواهر ، وعن هذا قال الغزالي : ( لا تُحرَّكُ عقائد العامَّةِ ، ويُتركونَ على ما هم عليه ((۱) ؛ : لأنَّ السنَّةَ مضَتْ بعدم البحث عن الضَّمائر ، وأنَّها إنَّما تنكشفُ في الآخرةِ يومَ تُبلَى السرائر ، وإِنَّما يجبُ بثُّ العلمِ لِمَنْ سأَلَهُ وكان أهلاً لهُ ، لا لِمَنْ أعرض عنه أو لم يكن أهلاً له ، ويعني والله أعلمُ : ما لم يَظهرِ المُنكَرُ في عقائدهم كزمننا هذا ، فيجب تغيير المنكر والتلطُّفُ تعليمهم الحق بما تسعُهُ عقولهم ، وقد جعل الله سبحانَهُ في الألفاظِ والأدلَّةِ سعةً ، فكلُّ يُخاطَبُ على قدر فهمِهِ ، والله المستعان
And as for what the proponent of the validity of taqlid is deluded by — the sufficiency for the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, and his Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, of applying the rulings of Islam and raising/ceasing fighting merely by pronouncing the two words of faith, without them investigating into consciences — there is no proof in that; because that is only from the category of applying rulings based on indications and outward appearances, and that is not our subject. Rather, our subject is what is between the servant and his Lord, and what saves him from eternal abiding with the rest of the disbelievers in the Fire. And the Prophet, peace be upon him, applied the rulings of Islam to those about whom it was certain [؟] that they had disbelief among the hypocrites, and that did not indicate that they were like that in the afterlife. And to this meaning I pointed by my saying: 'for verily they [worldly rulings] are not salvific in the afterlife'; meaning: as for this world, its rulings are built on outward appearances. And about this, al-Ghazali said: 'The creeds of the common people should not be disturbed, and they are left as they are' because the Sunnah has proceeded with not investigating inner consciences, and that they are only unveiled in the afterlife on the day when secrets are exposed. And it is only obligatory to disseminate knowledge to the one who asks for it and is qualified for it, not to the one who turns away from it or is not qualified for it. And he means, and Allah knows best, as long as manifest error has not appeared in their creeds, as in our time, then it is obligatory to change the wrong and gently teach them the truth according to the capacity of their intellects. And Allah has placed capacity in words and evidences, so everyone is addressed according to his level of understanding. And Allah is the source of help.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage addresses a common objection: if the Prophet and Companions accepted people upon mere verbal confession without investigation, doesn't that make taqlid acceptable? The author refutes this by distinguishing between worldly judgments (ahkam al-Islam) which are based on outward appearances (zawahir), and the true state of faith in the afterlife. The Prophet himself applied Islamic rulings to hypocrites who were inwardly disbelievers. Thus outward acceptance does not indicate salvation. The author then clarifies that this is why al-Ghazali said not to disturb the creeds of the common people: the Sunnah is not to investigate hearts. However, the author adds an important qualification: when manifest error (munkar) appears in their beliefs, as in the author's own time, it becomes obligatory to teach them gently. This shows that the principle of not disturbing common beliefs is conditional on absence of clear deviation.

Key Terms
الظواهر (zawahir)
السرائر (sara'ir)
المنكر (munkar)
العقائد (aqa'id)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because the Prophet accepted people's outward Islam without scrutiny, taqlid is sufficient for salvation.
Correction: This passage clarifies that worldly acceptance does not guarantee afterlife salvation; true faith requires investigation, and teaching is obligatory when error appears.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the Prophet's acceptance of hypocrites' outward Islam does not justify taqlid for salvation?
A) Because the hypocrites were not true believers.
B) Because worldly rulings are based on outward appearances and do not affect the afterlife.
C) Because taqlid is always forbidden.
D) Because the Prophet did not investigate anyone.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's acceptance of outward faith does not imply taqlid suffices for salvation; true faith requires investigation, and teaching becomes obligatory when manifest error appears in beliefs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi says that what saves a person from the Fire is what is between him and his Lord, not outward appearances. Are you more concerned with how you appear to others in your religious practice or with the reality of your faith before Allah? What specific step will you take today to ensure your inner faith matches your outer profession?
?
The Prophet treated hypocrites as Muslims in this world, but they were doomed in the afterlife. Is there any hypocrisy in your heart that you have been hiding behind outward acts of worship? What is one hidden doubt or negligence you need to confront and repent from?
?
Sanusi qualifies al-Ghazali's principle by saying teaching becomes obligatory when manifest error appears. Are you aware of any manifest error in your own beliefs or practices that you have ignored because you didn't want to be disturbed? What is it, and what will you do about it today?

Outward Rulings Do Not Prove Inner Salvation
Sanusi argues that outward acceptance does not guarantee inner salvation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Prophet's practice of accepting people based on outward utterance does not prove that such people are saved in the afterlife, and why this does not justify taqlid in creed.

Core Idea
Outward acceptance is for this world; inner faith is for the afterlife.

Many argue that since the Prophet accepted people just by saying the shahada, blind faith is enough. Sanusi corrects this: the Prophet also treated hypocrites as Muslims outwardly, while knowing their inner disbelief. This shows that outward rulings do not reflect inner salvation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note how Sanusi contrasts the rulings of this world with the afterlife.
The phrase 'إجراء الأحكام على المظان والظواهر' is key.
2
Identify the proof from hypocrites
Notice that Sanusi uses the Prophet's treatment of hypocrites as evidence. Why does this invalidate the claim that outward acceptance is proof of inner salvation?
The hypocrites were outwardly Muslims but inwardly disbelievers.
3
Understand the author's statement
Sanusi alludes to his earlier statement 'they are not salvific in the afterlife'. Explain why outward rulings are based on appearances, not inner reality.
This connects to the distinction between this world and the next.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عليه وسلم وأصحابه رضي الله عنهم بإجراء أحكام الإسلام ورفع وسلَّمَ القتالِ بمجرَّدِ النطق بكلمتي الإيمانِ مِنْ غيرِ بحث منهـم علـى السرائر . . فلا دليل فيهِ ؛ لأنَّ ذلكَ إنَّما هو مِنْ بابِ إجراء الأحكام على المظان والظواهر ، وليس كلامنا فيهِ ، وإِنَّما كلامنا فيما بينَ العبدِ وربَّهِ ، وفيما ينجيهِ مِنَ الخلودِ معَ سائرِ الكفرة في النار ، وقد أجرى النبيُّ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ أحكام الإسلام على مَنْ قُطِعَ بأردا كفرٍ فِيهِ مِنَ المنافقين ، ولم يدلّ ذلك على أنَّهم كذلك في الآخرة ، وإلى هذا المعنى أشرتُ بقولي : ( فإنَّها غيرُ مُخلَّصةٍ في الآخرةِ ) ؛ أي : وأما في الدنيا : فمبنى أحكامها على الظواهر ، وعن هذا قال الغزالي : ( لا تُحرَّكُ عقائد العامَّةِ ، ويُتركونَ على ما هم عليه ((۱) ؛ : لأنَّ السنَّةَ مضَتْ بعدم البحث عن الضَّمائر ، وأنَّها إنَّما تنكشفُ في الآخرةِ يومَ تُبلَى السرائر ، وإِنَّما يجبُ بثُّ العلمِ لِمَنْ سأَلَهُ وكان أهلاً لهُ ، لا لِمَنْ أعرض عنه أو لم يكن أهلاً له ، ويعني والله أعلمُ : ما لم يَظهرِ المُنكَرُ في عقائدهم كزمننا هذا ، فيجب تغيير المنكر والتلطُّفُ تعليمهم الحق بما تسعُهُ عقولهم ، وقد جعل الله سبحانَهُ في الألفاظِ والأدلَّةِ سعةً ، فكلُّ يُخاطَبُ على قدر فهمِهِ ، والله المستعان
and his Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, by applying the rulings of Islam and raising [i.e., refraining from] fighting merely upon utterance of the two words of faith, without examining their inner secrets. So there is no proof in that; because that is merely from the category of applying rulings based on assumptions and outward appearances, and our discussion is not about that. Rather, our discussion is about what is between the servant and his Lord, and about what saves him from eternal [punishment] with the rest of the disbelievers in the Fire. The Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, applied the rulings of Islam to those about whom it was certain that they were disbelievers among the hypocrites, and that did not indicate that they would be [deemed] likewise in the afterlife. It is to this meaning that I alluded by my saying: 'Indeed, they are not salvific in the afterlife.' That is: as for this world, the basis of its rulings is outward appearances. About this, al-Ghazali said: 'Do not disturb the beliefs of the common people, and leave them as they are,' because the sunna has continued with not investigating consciences, and they will only be disclosed in the afterlife on the day when secrets are tested. Knowledge should only be spread to those who ask for it and are qualified for it, not to those who turn away from it or are not qualified for it. He means, and Allah knows best, as long as open error does not appear in their beliefs, like in our time. Then it is obligatory to change the wrong and gently teach them the truth to the extent that their intellects can accommodate. Allah, exalted, has made a capacity in words and proofs, so each person is addressed according to the measure of his understanding. And Allah is the one whose help is sought.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage argues against using the Prophet's outward acceptance of people as evidence that taqlid (blind following) in creed is sufficient. The author (Sanusi) explains that the Prophet applied Islamic rulings based on the mere utterance of the shahada, without investigating inner convictions. However, this does not prove that such people are saved in the afterlife, because the Prophet also applied rulings to hypocrites who were inwardly disbelievers. Thus, outward acceptance does not guarantee inner salvation. The author then clarifies that his own earlier statement ('Indeed, they are not salvific in the afterlife') refers to the afterlife, while in this world rulings are based on appearances. He supports this with a quote from al-Ghazali, who advises not to disturb the common people's beliefs unless open error appears. The author adds a caveat: if error becomes manifest (as in his own time, he suggests), then it becomes obligatory to gently teach the truth. This shows that the principle of leaving common people alone is not absolute—it applies only when there is no open wrong belief. The passage emphasizes the capacity of language and proofs to address different levels of understanding.

الظواهر
السرائر
الآخرة
المنافقون

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Prophet's acceptance of people based on shahada means that taqlid is sufficient and inner faith is not required.
Correction: The Prophet accepted hypocrites outwardly, so outward acceptance does not guarantee inner salvation; inner faith is needed for the afterlife.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why does the Prophet's practice of accepting people based on outward faith not prove that taqlid is sufficient?
A) Because the Prophet never accepted anyone without testing them.
B) Because the hypocrites were also accepted outwardly but were inwardly disbelievers.
C) Because the Companions performed ijtihad for all rulings.
D) Because the Qur'an explicitly forbids taqlid.

Key Takeaway
Outward rulings of this world are based on appearances and do not guarantee salvation in the afterlife; true faith is required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been content with merely appearing Muslim in front of others while your heart is negligent? If you were to face your Lord tonight, would your outward deeds alone be enough, or is there a gap between your public Islam and your private faith? Identify one specific area where you can align your inner state with your outward practice starting today.
?
The Prophet treated hypocrites as Muslims outwardly, yet they are doomed. What if you are like a hypocrite in some aspect—performing the actions of Islam without the sincerity of faith? What single habit or relationship reveals whether your Islam is merely outward? Commit to a concrete change this week.
?
If you died now, would you be saved because you appear Muslim, or because you truly believe? This lesson says outward rulings are for this world, but salvation requires inner faith. What does your prayer life, your charity, or your speech reveal about the state of your heart? Set a five-minute daily check-in to examine your intentions.

The Principle of Not Disturbing Common Beliefs
Sanusi quotes al-Ghazali on not disturbing common beliefs and qualifies it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle expressed by al-Ghazali about not disturbing common people's beliefs, and the condition when it becomes obligatory to correct open error.

Core Idea
Leave common beliefs alone, unless open error appears—then gently teach.

Sanusi quotes al-Ghazali's famous advice not to disturb the beliefs of the common people. He then adds an important qualification: this applies only as long as no open error appears. In his own time, error had become manifest, making it obligatory to gently teach the truth. This lesson explores that balance.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ghazali quote
Read the Arabic quote from al-Ghazali: 'Do not disturb the beliefs of the common people...' and its translation. What is the reasoning behind this?
The reason given is that the sunna has continued without investigating consciences.
2
Identify the condition
Sanusi adds a condition: 'as long as open error does not appear in their beliefs, like in our time.' Why does he mention 'our time'?
He implies that in his era, error had become widespread, changing the ruling.
3
Understand the obligation to teach
Once error is manifest, it becomes obligatory to change the wrong and gently teach the truth. Note the emphasis on gentleness and addressing according to the person's capacity.
The passage ends with the capacity of language and proofs.
