Three Arguments for Taqlid and Beginning of Refutation
Sanusi enumerates three arguments for taqlid and begins refuting the third.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main arguments used to justify taqlid in creed and the author's initial refutation strategy, particularly the charge of begging the question against the third argument.

Core Idea
Defenders of taqlid use flawed proofs; the third argument commits circular reasoning.

Sanusi presents three arguments commonly used by those who defend blind following in matters of creed. After listing them, he begins his refutation, first dismissing the third as a logical fallacy. This passage sets the stage for a systematic critique of taqlid and a defense of rational reflection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Note the structure: enumeration of arguments, then a dismissive remark, followed by the beginning of refutation of the third argument.
The refutation is incomplete in this excerpt.
2
Identify the three arguments
List each argument and its supporting evidence: (1) Companions died without kalam terms, (2) reports from Salaf and scholars favoring simple faith, (3) empirical observation of stronger faith in muqallids.
3
Analyze the author's initial response
Notice that Sanusi declares the arguments corrupt 'لا يخفى فساد' and immediately targets the third argument as a case of begging the question (المصادرة على المطلوب). Ask yourself why this is a fallacy.
The author assumes that the majority of imams hold that reflection is necessary or that the comparison is flawed.
4
Reflect on the incomplete refutation
The passage ends mid-sentence. Consider what might be needed to complete the refutation and how this connects to the defense of kalam.
This lesson only covers the beginning of the refutation; the full argument is continued elsewhere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تفنيد شُبه القائلين بصحة التقليدِ ; يعني في واحتج بعضُهم ممَّنْ يميلُ إلى صحة القول بالتقليد ، بل ويرى رجحانَهُ على درجة الاجتهاد والنظر في علم التوحيد . . بأَوْجُهِ : أحدها : أنَّا نقطعُ أنَّ أبا بكر وعمر وسائرَ الصحابة رضيَ اللهُ عن جميعهم ماتوا ولم يعرفوا الجوهر والعَرَضَ ، ونُقِلَ عن الأستاذ ابن فُورَكَ أنَّه قال : ( لو لم يدخل الجنةَ التي عرضُها السماوات والأرض إلا مَنْ يعرفُ الجوهر والعرض.. لبقيَتْ خالية ) . الثاني : حُكِيَ عن بعض السلفِ أَنَّهُ قالَ : ( عليكم بدينِ العجائز ) ، وحُكِيَ عنِ الإمامِ الفخرِ أَنَّهُ قالَ عند موتِهِ : ( اللهم ؛ إيمان العجائز )، وقال عمرُ بنُ عبدِ العزيز رضي الله عنه لرجل سألَهُ عنِ الأهواء : ( عليك بدينِ الصبي الذي في الكُتَابِ، ودينِ الأعرابي ، ودع ما سواهما ) الثالثُ : أنَّا نجد بعض المقلّدينَ أقوى إيماناً وأرسخَ اعتقاداً ممَّنْ نظر في علم التوحيد . قلتُ : لا يخفى فساد ما تمسَّكَ بِهِ على كلِّ مُوفَّقٍ . أَمَّا الثالثُ - وهو رجحان إيمانِ بعض المقلّدينَ على إيمَانِ مَنْ نظر - : فهو مِنَ المصادرة على المطلوبِ ؛ لأنَّ جمهور الأئمة يرون
Refutation of the doubts of those who claim the validity of taqlid. Some of those who incline to the validity of the claim of taqlid— rather, who see its superiority over the level of ijtihad and reflection in the science of tawhid— argued with several points: First: We are certain that Abu Bakr, 'Umar, and the rest of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with all of them, died without knowing substance (al-jawhar) and accident (al-'arad). And it was narrated from the teacher Ibn Furak that he said: 'If only those who know substance and accident would enter the Garden whose width is the heavens and the earth, it would remain empty.' Second: It was narrated from some of the Salaf that he said: 'Hold fast to the religion of old women (din al-'aja'iz).' And it was narrated from Imam al-Fakhr that he said at his death: 'O Allah, the faith of old women.' And 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz, may Allah be pleased with him, said to a man who asked him about sects: 'Hold fast to the religion of the boy in the school, and the religion of the Bedouin, and leave what is besides them.' Third: We find that some muqallids (blind followers) have stronger faith and firmer belief than those who reflected in the science of tawhid. I say: The corruption of what they have clung to is not hidden from every divinely guided person. As for the third— which is the superiority of faith of some muqallids over the faith of those who reflected— it is a begging of the question (musadara 'ala al-matlub); because the majority of the imams hold...
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments used by proponents of taqlid (blind following) to support their view that simple faith without theological reflection is sufficient and even superior. The author (Sanusi) then begins his refutation.

1. First argument: The Companions, including Abu Bakr and 'Umar, died without knowing technical philosophical terms like 'substance' (al-jawhar) and 'accident' (al-'arad). This is supported by a quote from Ibn Furak that if only those who know these terms entered Paradise, it would be empty. The implication is that such deep knowledge is unnecessary.

2. Second argument: Reports from early authorities (al-Salaf) stating to hold to the 'religion of old women', and from Imam al-Fakhr (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) wishing for the 'faith of old women' at death. Additionally, 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz advised a questioner to stick to the simple faith of a schoolboy or a Bedouin, avoiding theological speculation.

3. Third argument: Empirical observation that some muqallids have stronger faith and firmer conviction than those who study kalam (theology). This is presented as evidence that simple faith is better.

After listing these arguments, the author (Sanusi) says: 'I say: The corruption of what they have clung to is not hidden from every divinely guided person.' Then he begins refuting the third argument specifically, calling it a case of begging the question (musadara 'ala al-matlub), because the majority of imams (presumably hold that reflection is necessary or that the comparison is flawed). The text cuts off here, so the full refutation is not included in this window. The author's method is to first present the opponents' strongest proofs, then dismantle them one by one.

Key Terms
التقليد
الجوهر
العرض
دين العجائز
إيمان العجائز
المصادرة على المطلوب
الاجتهاد
النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because some early scholars or simple people had strong faith without theological training, study of theology is harmful or unnecessary.
Correction: This passage shows that these claims are flawed and require refutation; the author begins to expose logical fallacies in them, particularly the third argument's circular reasoning.

Checkpoint
What is the third argument used by defenders of taqlid according to this passage?
A) The Companions did not know substance and accident.
B) The Salaf advised following the religion of old women.
C) Some muqallids have stronger faith than those who studied tawhid.
D) The Prophet accepted people's faith without investigation.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi critiques arguments for taqlid by exposing their flawed premises, particularly the third as a circular argument, though the full refutation is not present here.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You may rely on a faith inherited from your family, never questioning it. But Sanusi warns that arguments for such blind reliance are flawed. If your faith were ever genuinely challenged, could you defend it with reasons, or would you fall back on 'that's what I was taught'? What would it cost you to actually examine the foundations of your belief?
?
The third argument claims that some muqallids have stronger faith. But Sanusi calls it circular: the argument assumes that strong faith in a muqallid proves taqlid is right. Have you ever assumed that because someone appears devout, their path must be correct? What hidden assumptions do you carry about what makes faith 'strong'? Are you willing to question even the most sincere examples around you?
?
The 'religion of old women' is praised, but is your faith as simple as a child's, or have you never moved beyond that? Are you content with a faith that has never been tested by doubts? What is one belief you hold that you could not defend if challenged? And if you cannot defend it, is it truly your belief, or just a comfortable habit?

Three Objections Against Kalam
Sanusi lists three objections against the necessity of kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three common objections raised against the necessity of theological reasoning (kalam) and how they appeal to authority.

Core Idea
Objections to kalam often appeal to the simple faith of early Muslims and common people.

Sanusi begins by presenting three objections that critics of kalam often raise. These objections appeal to the examples of the Companions, the Salaf, and ordinary muqallids to argue that deep theological reasoning is unnecessary. Understanding these objections is essential before seeing how Sanusi refutes them.

Guided Path
1
Read the three objections
Read the Arabic passage listing the three objections. Notice how each objection uses an authority figure (Companions, Salaf, common people).
The objections are presented without refutation at this stage.
2
Identify the key figures
Note the names: Abu Bakr, Umar, Ibn Furak, al-Fakhr al-Razi, Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz. Think about why their examples are used.
These figures are cited to show that even great Muslims didn't engage in kalam.
3
Reflect on the common thread
All three objections share the idea that deep knowledge (like knowing jawhar and arad) is unnecessary for faith. Consider why this might be appealing.

أحدها : أنا نقطع أن أبا بكر وعمر وسائر الصحابة رضي الله عن جميعهم ماتوا ولم يعرفوا الجوهر والعرض، ونقل عن الأستاذ ابن فورك أنه قال : ( لو لم يدخل الجنة التي عرضها السماوات والأرض إلا من يعرف الجوهر والعرض.. لبقت خالية ) . الثاني : حكي عن بعض السلف أنه قال : ( عليكم بدين العجائز )، وحكي عن الإمام الفخر أنه قال عند موته : ( اللهم إيمان العجائز ) وقال عمر بن عبد العزيز رضي الله عنه لرجل سأله عن الأهواء : ( عليك بدين الصبي الذي في الكتاب، ودين الأعرابي، ودع ما سواهما ) الثالث : أنا نجد بعض المقلدين أقوى إيمانا وأرسخ اعتقادا ممن نظر في علم التوحيد . قلت : لا يخفى فساد ما تمسك به على كل موفق . أما الثالث - وهو رجحان إيمان بعض المقلدين على إيمان من نظر - : فهو من المصادرة على المطلوب ؛ لأن جمهور الأئمة يرون وجوب النظر وتحريم الاقتصار على التقليد، وبعضهم يرى أن لا إيمان للمقلد أصلا، فكيف يدعي رجحانه ؟! وأيضا فمما لا يدخل تحت فهم عاقل : أن الجزم المستند إلى مجرد التقليد - ومن لازمه قبول احتمال النقيض - يكون مساويا للجزم الذي أنتجته البراهين بحيث لا يحتمل النقيض بوجه من الوجوه .
First: We are certain that Abu Bakr, 'Umar, and the rest of the Companions, may God be pleased with all of them, died without knowing the substance and the accident. And it was transmitted from al-Ustadh Ibn Furak that he said: 'If only those who know the substance and the accident were to enter Paradise, the breadth of which is the heavens and the earth, it would remain empty.' Second: It is related from some of the Salaf that he said: 'Stick to the religion of old women.' And it is related from al-Imam al-Fakhr (al-Razi) that he said at the time of his death: 'O God, the faith of old women.' And 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz, may God be pleased with him, said to a man who asked him about sects: 'Stick to the religion of the child in the school, and the religion of the bedouin, and leave what is besides them.' Third: We find some blind followers (muqallids) with stronger faith and more rooted belief than those who have looked into the science of tawhid. I say: The corruption of that which they have clung to is not hidden to every divinely guided person. As for the third — that is, the superiority of the faith of some muqallids over the faith of those who have reflected — it is a begging of the question (musadara 'ala al-matlub); because the majority of the imams hold that reflection (nazar) is obligatory and that limiting oneself to taqlid is forbidden, and some of them hold that there is no faith for the muqallid at all. So how can one claim its superiority? Moreover, it is not within the understanding of a rational person that a certainty based on mere taqlid — and entailing the acceptance of the possibility of the opposite — would be equal to a certainty produced by proofs (barahin) which admits no possibility of the opposite in any way.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents three objections commonly raised against the necessity of kalam (theological reasoning) and then refutes the third in detail. The first objection: The early Muslims, including Abu Bakr, 'Umar, and all the Companions, died without knowing the technical terms 'substance' (jawhar) and 'accident' ('arad) — the basic concepts of kalam. Ibn Furak is quoted as saying that if Paradise required knowledge of these, it would be empty. The second objection: Some Salaf advised sticking to the 'religion of old women', and Fakhr al-Din al-Razi allegedly prayed for 'the faith of old women'. 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz similarly advised sticking to the religion of children and bedouins — implying simple faith without intellectual inquiry. The third objection: We often find that blind followers (muqallids) have stronger and more rooted faith than those who have studied tawhid. The author then says the corruption of these arguments is obvious to the divinely guided. He focuses on the third: claiming superiority for the muqallid's faith is a begging of the question, because the majority of imams hold that reflection (nazar) is obligatory and taqlid is forbidden, and some deny the muqallid any faith at all. So how can one claim it is superior? Moreover, the certainty from taqlid always admits the possibility of the opposite (since it is based on authority, not proof), while certainty from demonstration excludes that possibility entirely. They cannot be equal.

Key Terms
جوهر
عرض
تقليد
إيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Simple faith without understanding is equal or superior to reasoned faith.
Correction: Sanusi later shows that the muqallid's faith admits doubt, while demonstrative faith is certain.

Checkpoint
According to the first objection, what did the Companions not know?
A) The essence of Allah
B) The substance and the accident
C) The pillars of faith
D) The Quranic verses

Key Takeaway
The three objections appeal to the faith of Companions, old women, and muqallids to argue that theological reasoning is unnecessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know the three objections. Which one, in your quietest moments, resonates with you? Have you ever felt that studying ‘aqeedah is unnecessary or even harmful to your faith? Write down exactly when that feeling has surfaced, and then read again the phrase ‘blind followers (muqallids) with stronger faith.’ Does that description fit you? If so, are you ready to step into the discomfort of learning, or will you cling to that simplicity as an excuse? Pray for courage to seek true certainty.
?
The Companions died without knowing jawhar and ‘arad. But they lived with the Prophet. You do not. So when you tell yourself that simple faith is enough, are you comparing yourself to them or to old women? Be honest: Are you using their piety as a shield against your own responsibility to know? What specific argument from this lesson forces you to admit that you need more than imitation? Tonight, decide one step you will take to move from taqlid to true knowledge, and commit to it in your journal.
?
‘Umar ibn ‘Abd al-‘Aziz told a man to stick to the religion of a child or a bedouin. But he himself was a scholar. Are you taking his advice for yourself, or are you quoting him while hiding from study? Look at the third objection—the muqallids with strong faith. That faith might be real, but is it defended? If you were challenged by a determined atheist, would your faith crumble? That is the mirror this passage holds up to you. What will you do about it starting tomorrow?

Refutation of the Third Objection
Sanusi refutes the third objection as a logical fallacy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that muqallids have superior faith is logically flawed and constitutes a begging of the question.

Core Idea
The muqallid's faith admits possibility of error, while demonstrative certainty does not.

After listing the objections, Sanusi focuses on the third objection—that muqallids often have stronger faith than those who study tawhid. He argues that this claim is a fallacy known as begging the question (musadara 'ala al-matlub) and that the certainty of taqlid is inherently inferior to that of demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Read the Arabic passage where Sanusi refutes the third objection. Notice the terms 'musadara ala al-matlub' and 'barahin'.
The refutation is concise but packed with logical reasoning.
2
Understand the logical fallacy
Sanusi says the claim is 'musadara ala al-matlub'—it assumes what needs to be proved: that taqlid is valid and that muqallids have faith.
The majority of imams hold that nazar is obligatory and taqlid is forbidden.
3
Compare certainties
The certainty from taqlid always admits the possibility of the opposite, while certainty from proofs (barahin) excludes that possibility entirely. Therefore, they cannot be equal.
This shows that the muqallid's faith is inferior in terms of certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

Miraculous Knowledge and the Duty of Nazar
Sanusi clarifies that miraculous knowledge does not remove nazar's obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the possibility of miraculous knowledge does not lift the obligation of nazar for those who have not been granted such a station.

Core Idea
Miraculous knowledge is an exception, not the rule; nazar remains obligatory for most.

Sanusi anticipates an objection: if God can grant knowledge directly, why is nazar required? He responds by distinguishing between the rare saint who receives miraculous knowledge and the ordinary person who must follow the customary path. This passage shows how the possibility of miracles does not undermine the general duty of reflection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Notice the distinction between the saint (wali) and the muqallid.
2
Identify the two cases
The passage contrasts two scenarios: the saint who receives knowledge through kharq al-‘ada, and the ordinary muqallid. Understand how the saint is not a muqallid but has genuine knowledge.
3
Understand why the exception does not negate the rule
Sanusi argues that such miracles are rare and reserved for saints, so they do not remove the obligation of nazar for others. The customary and legislated path remains nazar and learning.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد تحصل المعرفة من غير نظر خرقا للعادة . ولعله أراد بعض من لم ينظر من أولياء الله، وخرقت في حقه ووهب له من المعارف ما لا يتوصل إليه بالنظر، حتى صارت علوم الناظرين بالنسبة إلى ما أعطي من العلوم كلا شيء، وإذا أراد هذا فليس هو من محل النزاع؛ لأن نزاعنا في المقلد، وهذا الذي ذكر ليس بمقلد، بل هو كالناظر في أن الحاصل له علم لا اعتقاد، وتوقف العلم غير الضروري على النظر إنما هو بحسب العادة، ويجوز في قدرة الله تعالى أن يجعل العلوم النظرية لمن شاء ضرورية؛ بحيث لا يفتقر في تحصيلها إلى نظر، إلا أن تجويز مثل هذا الخارق الذي لم يعط إلا للنادر من الأولياء لا يسقط وجوب النظر في حق من لم يحصل له هذا المقام، والذي جرت به العادة وأمر به الشرع تحصيل العلوم من طريقها المألوف؛ وهو الاجتهاد في النظر، والتعلم من.
Knowledge may be obtained without reflection (nazar), as a breaking of the customary (kharaq li-l-‘ada). And perhaps he means some of the saints (awliya') who did not reflect, for whom the customary was broken and they were granted forms of knowledge not attainable through reflection, such that the sciences of those who reflect are, relative to what they were given, as nothing. If this is what he intends, then it is not the subject of dispute; for our dispute concerns the muqallid, but the one mentioned is not a muqallid; rather he is like one who reflects in that what he obtains is knowledge, not belief (i‘tiqad). And the dependence of non-necessary knowledge on reflection is only according to custom (bi-hasab al-‘ada). It is possible within the power of God Almighty to make theoretical sciences (al-‘ulum al-nazariyya) necessary (daruriyya) for whomever He wills, so that they do not need reflection to acquire them. However, permitting such a miraculous occurrence (kharq) that is not granted except to the rare among the saints does not remove the obligation of reflection for those who have not attained this station. And that which custom has established and the Law (shar‘) has commanded is the acquisition of sciences through their familiar path: that is, striving in reflection and learning from.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential counterargument: perhaps knowledge of God can be obtained directly through divine favor (kharq al-‘ada) without reflection. The author acknowledges this possibility, but explains that such cases are exceptions for saints (awliya') and do not apply to the muqallid. The one who receives miraculous knowledge is not a muqallid; he has genuine knowledge, not mere belief, and is like one who has reflected. The dependence of non-necessary knowledge on reflection is only according to the normal course of custom (al-mu‘tad). God can make theoretical knowledge necessary if He wills. However, this does not invalidate the general obligation of reflection. The path that custom and the Law have commanded is the familiar one: striving in reflection and learning from teachers.

Key Terms
خرق العادة
ولي
علم ضروري
علم نظري
عادة
شرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that since God can grant knowledge without nazar, nazar is not required.
Correction: Such miracles are rare and only for saints; they do not remove the obligation of nazar for the vast majority.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main point regarding those who receive miraculous knowledge (kharq al-‘ada)?
A) They are excused from the obligation of nazar.
B) They are not muqallids and their case does not negate nazar for others.
C) They are better than those who reflect.
D) They have mere belief (i‘tiqad), not knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The exceptional case of saintly miraculous knowledge does not remove the obligation of nazar for those not granted such a station.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever hoped for a spiritual shortcut to certainty in your faith, avoiding the hard work of studying and reflecting? What does this lesson say about that desire—and what is the risk you are taking?
?
If you were granted miraculous knowledge tomorrow, would you still be obligated to the path of nazar? The author says no—but are you willing to bet your soul on being one of those rare saints? Or will you commit to the familiar path of effort and learning?
?
When you feel overwhelmed by the difficulty of studying aqeedah, do you ever think, 'I wish God would just give me knowledge directly'? After reading this passage, how does the author's reminder about the customary path reshape your responsibility toward your own learning?

The Effort Required in Seeking Knowledge
Sanusi cites hadith and Quran on the necessity of effort in seeking knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that seeking knowledge demands hardship, travel, and perseverance, as demonstrated by hadith, Quran, and the examples of the righteous predecessors.

Core Idea
Knowledge demands hardship; ease is not its path.

Sanusi begins by emphasizing that true knowledge is not attained through comfort. He marshals proofs from the Prophet’s sayings, the Quran, and the practice of the early Muslims to show that effort is essential. This lesson explores those proofs and their implications for the student of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the hadith proofs.
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the three hadiths cited: 'Knowledge cannot be attained with bodily ease,' 'Seek knowledge even in China,' and 'Knowledge is only through learning.'
These hadiths set the foundation that knowledge requires toil.
2
Reflect on the Quranic commands to 'take with strength.'
Consider the verses addressed to Yahya and Musa, commanding them to take the Book/tablets 'with strength.' What does this strength refer to in the context of knowledge?
The verses imply earnest effort and commitment.
3
Consider the examples of the salaf and Prophet Musa.
The passage mentions that the salaf would travel a month for a single benefit, and Musa endured fatigue seeking knowledge from Khidr. Reflect on how these examples reinforce the message.
Even the greatest prophets were not exempt from hardship in seeking knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العلماء ، والتزام التعب في الدرس ، والرحلة في طلب الفوائدِ ، وقد رُوِيَ في الحديث : ( لا يُستطاعُ العلم براحة الجسم ) (۱) ، و أطْلُبُوا الْعِلْمَ وَلَوْ بِالصِّينِ (۲) ، وورد : « إِنَّمَا الْعِلْمُ بِالتَّعَلُّم (۳) وقال الله تعالى لنبيه يحيى عليهِ السلامُ : يَيَحْيَى خُذِ الْكِتَابَ بقوة ﴾ [مريم : ۱۲] ، وقال لكليمه موسى عليهِ السلامُ : ﴿ وَكَتَبْنَا لَهُ فِي الْأَلْوَاحِ مِن كُلِّ شَيْءٍ .... إلى قولِهِ : ﴿ فَخُذْهَا بِقُوَّةٍ ﴾ [الأعراف : ﴾ ١٤٥ ] ، وقال جل وعلا : ﴿فَلَوْلَا نَفَرَ مِن كُلِّ فِرْقَةٍ مِّنْهُمْ طَائِفَةٌ ... [التوبة : ١٢٢] الآية . وكانَ السلف الصالحُ يرتحلُ أحدهم لطلب الفائدة الواحدة مسير شهرٍ ، ولقد سافر كليم الله موسى عليهِ السلامُ مَعَ مَا أُعطِيَ مِنْ علمٍ كلٌّ شيء للقاء الخضر عليهِ السلامُ حتى مَسَّهُ التعب في ذلكَ ، وقال : لَقَدْ لَقِينَا مِن سَفَرِنَا هَذَا نَصَبا) [الكهف : ٦٢] (٤)
The scholars, and committing to hardship in study, and traveling to seek benefits. And it has been narrated in the hadith: 'Knowledge cannot be attained with bodily ease' (1), and 'Seek knowledge even in China' (2), and it is reported: 'Knowledge is only through learning' (3). And Allah the Exalted said to His prophet Yahya, peace be upon him: 'O Yahya, take the Book with strength' [Maryam: 12]. And He said to His interlocutor Musa, peace be upon him: 'And We wrote for him on the tablets of everything... until His saying: 'So take them with strength' [Al-A'raf: 145]. And He, Glorious and Exalted, said: 'So why did not a group from every faction among them go forth...' [At-Tawbah: 122] the verse. And the righteous predecessors (salaf) used to travel, one of them, to seek a single benefit for a month's journey. And indeed the interlocutor of Allah, Musa, peace be upon him, despite what he was given of all-encompassing knowledge, traveled to meet Khidr, peace be upon him, until hardship touched him in that, and he said: 'We have indeed met from this journey of ours fatigue' [Al-Kahf: 62] (4).
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage exhorts the seeker of knowledge to embrace hardship, travel, and perseverance. It begins by stating that scholars commit to hardship in study and travel for benefits. Then it cites three hadiths: 'Knowledge cannot be attained with bodily ease,' 'Seek knowledge even in China,' and 'Knowledge is only through learning.' Next, it quotes three Quranic verses: Allah tells Yahya to take the Book with strength; He tells Musa to take the tablets with strength; and the verse urging groups to go forth for knowledge. The passage then notes that the righteous predecessors would travel a month for a single benefit, and even Musa (despite his great knowledge) endured fatigue seeking knowledge from Khidr. The core message is that knowledge demands effort, and this effort is itself a virtue.

Key Terms
التعب في الدرس
الرحلة في طلب الفوائد
السلف الصالح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think knowledge can be attained without effort or hardship.
Correction: This passage shows that knowledge requires hardship, travel, and perseverance, as proven by hadith, Quran, and the examples of the salaf and prophets.

Checkpoint
What does the hadith 'لا يُستطاعُ العلم براحة الجسم' mean?
A) Knowledge is only for those with strong bodies.
B) Knowledge cannot be attained with bodily ease.
C) Knowledge is attained through rest.
D) Knowledge is only for the wealthy.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is not attained through ease; hardship, travel, and perseverance are essential.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you last studied something Islamic, did you choose the easy path or did you embrace discomfort—studying while tired, traveling, sacrificing leisure? What would change if you took the Book 'with strength' tomorrow?
?
Prophet Musa, despite immense knowledge, traveled and faced fatigue. What legitimate excuse do you have for not exerting more effort in your own pursuit of knowledge—whether through waking early, traveling to a teacher, or persevering in a difficult text?
?
If you knew that a month's journey was required to benefit from a single hadith, would you go? What does your hesitation reveal about your love for knowledge?

Disobedient Scholar vs Obedient Follower
Sanusi compares a sinful scholar and an obedient follower.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a scholar who disobeys is considered better than a blind follower who obeys, according to the majority of scholars.

Core Idea
Knowledge gives value to action; without it, action is void.

This passage addresses a common objection: if scholars sometimes sin, how can they be superior? Sanusi argues that knowledge itself confers a status that even sinful actions cannot erase, while blind obedience has no weight because the follower's faith is deemed invalid by most scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the strong claim: a disobedient scholar is better than an obedient blind follower.
2
Understand the reasoning
Sanusi gives two reasons: (1) the blind follower's faith is invalid according to the majority, so his actions count for nothing. (2) A little action with knowledge is better than much action without knowledge.
The word 'mukhalif' (disobedient) refers to sins of the limbs, not disbelief.
3
Consider the example of monks
Sanusi cites Christian monks and ignorant ascetics who undergo severe hardship but gain no benefit in the Hereafter because their actions lack true knowledge.
This illustrates the principle that action without knowledge is worthless.
4
Reflect on the lesson
The lesson is not to excuse sin, but to highlight the intrinsic value of knowledge. True knowledge elevates even imperfect practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ فضل العلم على العمل ]
ثم هذا العالم المخالفُ بالجوارح هو أحسن حالاً مِنَ المقلّدِ الموافق ؛ لأنَّ المقلد قال الجمهورُ بعدم صحةِ إيمانِهِ ، فلا يكونُ لهُ عملٌ ، ولَقليلُ العملِ معَ العلم أفضلُ مِنْ كثيرِ العمل بلا علم بل لا أثر للعمل الخالي عن العلم أصلاً ، وقد شَدَّدَ رهبانُ النصارى ومَنْ في معناهم مِنَ الجهلةِ على أنفسهم في الدنيا تشديداً عظيماً ، ومع ذلك لا ينفعهم شيئاً في الآخرةِ
ثم لو جئنا لِعَدِّ المحاسنِ والأعمال التي اتّصف بها أكثرُ العلماءِ مِنْ أئمة المسلمين ومشايخ الأولياء الذين هم قدوة المتقين وما لهم مِنَ العلومِ ، ثم بثّها تعليماً وتأليفاً وجهاداً لكلِّ مبطل حتى انقطعَ مِنْ كُلِّ جاهل ومبتدع التشرُّفُ إلى الاختلاسِ مِنَ الدين.. لغابَ في أدنى مَكْرُمةٍ لهم جميعُ أعمالِ عامَّةِ المسلمينَ ، لكنَّ مشاهدة المتشبهين بأهل العلم وليسوا منهم ، وعِزَّةَ وجودِ أهل العلم على الحقيقة .. هي التي جسَّرَتِ الجاهلَ بمناقبِ مَنْ مضى مِنْ أَئِمَّةِ المسلمين على ذكرِ هبي[؟] العامَّةِ في مَعرِضِ ذكرِ العلماء الراسخينَ ، رضيَ اللهُ تعالى عنهم ونفعنا بهم ، وحشرنا في زمرتهم
[Virtue of knowledge over action]
Then this scholar who disobeys with bodily faculties is in a better state than the blind follower who is obedient, because the majority (of scholars) said that the blind follower's faith is not valid, so he has no action. And a little action with knowledge is better than much action without knowledge. Rather, there is no effect for action devoid of knowledge at all. And the monks of the Christians and those like them among the ignorant have imposed on themselves severe hardship in this world, yet it will not benefit them at all in the Hereafter.
Then, if we were to enumerate the virtues and deeds that characterize most of the scholars – the imams of the Muslims and the shaykhs of the saints, who are the leaders of the pious, and what they possess of knowledge, and then their dissemination of it through teaching, authorship, and struggle against every falsehood, until every ignorant and innovator was cut off from aspiring to steal anything from the religion – then all the deeds of the common Muslims would be lost in comparison to even the slightest honour of theirs. But the sight of those who imitate the people of knowledge while not being among them, and the rarity of true people of knowledge – these are what have emboldened the ignorant to speak disparagingly of the past imams of the Muslims while speaking of the common people in the context of mentioning the firmly grounded scholars. May Allah be pleased with them, benefit us through them, and gather us in their group.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the dignity of knowledge against the objection that some scholars disobey God. The author begins by stating that even a scholar who disobeys (mukhalif bil-jawarih) is in a better state than a blind follower (muqallid) who obeys, because the majority of scholars hold that the blind follower's faith is invalid, so he has no valid actions to his credit. He then asserts that a little action combined with knowledge is better than much action without knowledge, and that action devoid of knowledge has no effect at all. As evidence, he cites the example of Christian monks and ignorant ascetics who impose severe hardship on themselves but gain nothing in the Hereafter. Then, to further demonstrate the superiority of scholars, he invites us to enumerate the good qualities and deeds of the majority of scholars—the imams and saints who spread knowledge through teaching, writing, and jihad against falsehood, to the extent that innovators and ignoramuses were prevented from stealing from religion. He claims that even the least of their merits outweighs all the deeds of ordinary Muslims. However, he notes that the sight of those who imitate scholars while not being truly learned, and the rarity of true scholars, have emboldened the ignorant to disparage the past imams when speaking of common people in the context of the firmly grounded scholars. The passage concludes with a prayer for the Salaf. The key idea is that knowledge has intrinsic value and that the collective contributions of true scholars are immense, so one should not judge the worth of knowledge by the shortcomings of some individuals.

Key Terms
عالم
مخالف
مقلّد
عمل خالٍ عن العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the author permits sin for scholars.
Correction: The author is not excusing sin but defending the intrinsic value of knowledge; even imperfect knowledge is better than blind obedience.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the obedient blind follower have no valid actions?
A) Because he disobeys in his heart
B) Because his faith is considered invalid by the majority
C) Because he lacks knowledge of the law
D) Because he follows a different school of thought

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is the foundation that makes actions meaningful; without it, even great effort is wasted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own religious practice, are you relying on knowledge or just following what you've been taught? What will you do to ensure your actions are grounded in understanding, not mere habit?
?
Think of a deed you have done that you thought was great. Now ask yourself: Was it based on knowledge? If not, how will you respond to this lesson’s warning that such actions have no effect?
?
The Christian monks endured severe hardship yet gained nothing. How do you know your own sacrifices and efforts are not similarly empty? What concrete step will you take today to build your actions on knowledge?

The Immense Merits of True Scholars
Sanusi enumerates scholars' merits and rebukes detractors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will appreciate the immense collective merits of true scholars and why their detractors are misguided.

Core Idea
The contributions of true scholars far outweigh all common acts of worship.

Sanusi now paints a picture of the overwhelming virtues of the true scholars—the imams and saints. He argues that their combined efforts in teaching, writing, and struggle have such a vast impact that even the smallest of their merits surpasses the deeds of all ordinary Muslims. He then diagnoses the cause of those who speak ill of them: the abundance of pretenders and the scarcity of genuine scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the hyperbole: the least honor of scholars outweighs all deeds of common people.
The word 'مشتبهون' refers to those who imitate scholars but are not truly learned.
2
Understand the enumeration
Sanusi lists the typical qualities of scholars: teaching, authorship, and jihad against falsehood. These have a far-reaching effect.
3
Identify the obstacle
Two factors lead people to disparage past imams: the sight of fake scholars and the rarity of true ones.
This explains why some ignorants are bold enough to criticize the great scholars.
4
Reflect on the prayer
The passage ends with a prayer for the Salaf, hoping to benefit from them and be gathered with them.
This shows the author's deep respect and connection to the early scholars.

The Intended Meaning of 'Stick to the Religion of Old Women'
Sanusi refutes the claim by explaining the true meaning of the Salaf's saying.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the true meaning of the Salaf's statement 'Stick to the religion of old women' and why it does not support blind taqlid but rather commands adherence to the consensus of the early Muslims and rejection of innovations.

Core Idea
The Salaf's saying commands sticking to their agreed creed, not rejecting knowledge.

A common objection to the study of kalam is that the Salaf reportedly told people to stick to the religion of old women, implying that rational inquiry is forbidden. But Sanusi shows that this interpretation is mistaken. The speaker actually meant to hold fast to what the righteous predecessors unanimously believed and to abandon the innovations that arose later. This lesson unpacks that explanation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the reported statement and Sanusi's response
Read the passage where Sanusi quotes the statement and immediately denies that it supports taqlid. Notice how he begins with 'There is no evidence in it.'
2
Understand the speaker's intention
Sanusi explains that the command is to adhere to what the Companions and Followers agreed upon, which even reached those incapable of independent inquiry like old women and children.
This shows the statement is about transmission of agreed knowledge, not prohibition of learning.
3
Recognize the second part: abandoning innovations
The statement also commands leaving what the innovators introduced, which had no existence in the Salaf's time.
This refutes the claim that the statement rejects all new ideas; it rejects only those contradicting the Salaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني - وهو ما حكاه عن بعض السلف مِنْ قوله : ( عليكم بدين العجائز ) - : فلا دليلَ فيهِ أيضاً على صحة التقليد ؛ لأنَّ مراد هذا القائل : الأمرُ بالتمسُّكِ بما اجتمعَ عليه السلف الصالحُ مِنَ الصحابة والتابعين حتى وصل علمُهُ إلى مَنْ ليسَ أهلاً للنظرِ ؛ كالعجائز ، والصبيان في الكتاب ، والأعراب أهل البذو ، وترك ما أحدثه المبتدعةُ مِنَ القدريَّةِ والمرجئة والجبرية والروافض وغيرِهم ممَّا لا وجود له في أعصارِ السلف الصالح خاصهم وعامهم ، وذكرُ أمثلة ذلك على الاستيفاء يطولُ ، ولنذكرِ البعض ليتبيَّنَ بهِ المراد : فمِنْ ذلكَ : ما أحدثه المعتزلة مِنْ تقييد إرادة الله جل وعلا بالطاعة ، وأنَّ الكفر والمعاصي لم يُرِدْها اللهُ عزَّ وجلَّ ، وإِنَّما العباد أوقعوا ما لم يُرِدْهُ اللهُ جلَّ وعزَّ ، ومعلوم أنَّ هذهِ ضلالةٌ لا مستند لها ، وإِنَّما الذي اشتهر في زمن السلف الصالح وتلقاه منهم الخلف ، ولَهِجَ بهِ الصغير والكبير ، والذكر والأنثى ، والحـرُّ والعبد ، والحاضر والبادي ، حتى صار كَأَنَّهُ معلومٌ مِنْ دينِ المسلمينَ ضرورةً ، يَلهَجُ بهِ مَنْ عرف معناهُ ومَنْ لم يعرف .. وقوع الكائنات كلها بإرادةِ اللهِ تعالى ، وأنَّ ما شاءَ اللهُ كانَ ، وما لم يشأ لم يكن ، إنَّ جهلة العصاة يعتذرونَ عن معاصيهم بإرادة الله تعالى منهم حتى ذلكَ ، ولو أرادَ اللهُ تعالى بهم خيراً لما عَصَوا . ونحو هذا : ما أنكرَهُ المعتزلة مِنْ جوازِ العَفْوِ عَمَّنْ ماتَ مُصِرّاً على المعاصي ، وإنكار الشفاعة له ، وإنكار خلْقِ الجنَّةِ والنار ، ومثل هذا كثير في العقائدِ ويدلُّ قطعاً على هذا التأويل الذي ذكرناه : إتيان عمر بنِ عبدِ العزيز بمثل هذا جواباً للسائل عن الأهواء ، فكأنَّهُ قالَ لَهُ : عليكَ الدين بما كانَ عليه السلفُ ، وتلقَّاهُ منهم الخلفُ ، ودع ما يناقضُ ذلك ممَّا أحدثه المبتدعة . بل نقولُ : هذهِ الألفاظ التي اغترَّ بها مَنْ مَالَ إِلى التقليدِ ، وحَذَرَ مِنَ النظرِ في التوحيدِ .. هي في الحقيقةِ حُجَّةٌ عليهِ لا له ؛ لأنَّ علماء السنَّةِ رضوانُ اللهِ عليهم إنَّما ألَّفوا في علم التوحيد ليبينوا للناس ما كانَ عليه السلف الصالح ، وصارَ لشهرته ووضوحه قبل ظهورِ البدع ديناً لعجائزهم وإمائِهم وأهْلِ بذوهم وصبيانِ كُتَّابِهم ، وزادوا بأنْ حصنوه بالبراهين العقليّةِ التي تنتهي إلى ضرورات العقل ، بحيثُ يخرجُ مَنْ أنكرها عن ديوان العقلاء ، وبالأدلَّةِ النقليَّةِ القطعية فيما تقبلُ فيهِ ، فهم رضي الله عنهم جعلوا على حرزِ دين الإسلام أسواراً لما قدمَتْ جيوشُ المبتدعة التي لا تُحصى كثرة تريد استلابَ ذلك الدين وإبداله بجهالات يهلِكُ مَن اتَّبَعَها . ثم لمَّا أَتَتِ المبتدعةُ بمعاولِ الشبهات لتهدم بها أسوار الأدلَّةِ ، وبسلالم الأوهام والتخيُّلاتِ لتتجاوز بها إلى حرز الدين.. بالغَتْ
As for the second - which is what he reported from some of the Salaf of his saying: 'Stick to the religion of old women' -: there is no evidence in it either for the correctness of taqlid (blind following); because the intention of this speaker is the command to adhere to what the righteous Salaf from the Companions and the Followers unanimously agreed upon, until its knowledge reached those who are not qualified for independent inquiry (nazar) — like old women, children in the elementary school, and the Bedouins, the people of the desert — and to abandon what the innovators (mubtadi'a) invented among the Qadariyya, the Murji'a, the Jabriyya, the Rawafid, and others, which had no existence in the eras of the righteous Salaf, both their elite and commoners. And the exhaustive mention of examples of that would be lengthy, so we will mention some so that the intended meaning becomes clear: Among that is what the Mu'tazila innovated of restricting the will of Allah, Glorified and Exalted, to obedience, and that disbelief and sins Allah, Mighty and Majestic, did not will them, but rather the servants brought about what Allah, Glorified and Exalted, did not will. And it is known that this is a misguidance with no basis. Rather, what was famous in the time of the righteous Salaf and was received from them by the successors (khalaf), and was uttered by young and old, male and female, free and slave, the present and the desert dweller — until it became as if it were known from the religion of the Muslims by necessity, uttered by those who understand its meaning and those who do not — is: the occurrence of all things by the will of Allah, Most High, and that what Allah wills comes to be, and what He does not will does not come to be. Indeed, the ignorant sinners excuse their sins by the will of Allah, Most High, from them for that, and if Allah, Most High, had willed good for them, they would not have sinned. And similar to this is what the Mu'tazila denied of the permissibility of pardon for one who dies persisting in sins, and the denial of intercession for him, and the denial of the creation of Paradise and Hell. And such matters are many in the creed. And what indicates decisively for this interpretation which we mentioned is the coming of 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz with something like this as an answer to the questioner about sectarian whims (ahwa'). As if he said to him: 'Stick to the religion according to what the Salaf were upon, and the successors received from them, and leave whatever contradicts that of what the innovators have invented.' Rather we say: These very expressions by which those who inclined to taqlid and warned against inquiry (nazar) in tawhid were deluded... are in reality a proof against him, not for him; because the scholars of the Ahl al-Sunna, may Allah be pleased with them, only authored works on the science of tawhid to explain to the people what the righteous Salaf were upon, and it became — due to its fame and clarity before the emergence of innovations — the religion of their old women, their female servants, their people of the desert, and the children of their elementary schools. And they further fortified it with rational proofs (barahin 'aqliyya) that terminate in intellectual necessities, such that one who denies them leaves the register of rational people, and with definitive transmitted proofs (adilla naqliyya qat'iyya) in matters where such are accepted. So they — may Allah be pleased with them — made around the fortress of the religion of Islam walls when the armies of the innovators — whose multitude cannot be counted — advanced, seeking to plunder that religion and replace it with ignorances by which those who follow them will perish. Then when the innovators came with pickaxes of doubts to demolish thereby the walls of proofs, and with ladders of illusions and fantasies to transcend them to the fortress of religion... they intensified.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the argument that the statement attributed to some Salaf, 'Stick to the religion of old women,' supports blind taqlid and prohibits kalam (theological inquiry). The author, following the method of Sanusi, argues that this statement is actually a proof for kalam, not against it.

The passage begins by addressing the second objection: the report that some Salaf said 'Stick to the religion of old women.' The author denies that this supports taqlid. He explains that the speaker intended to command adherence to what the righteous Salaf (Companions and Followers) agreed upon, which eventually reached those who are not capable of independent inquiry, such as old women, schoolchildren, and Bedouins. The point is to stick to the consensus of the Salaf and to abandon the innovations introduced by groups like the Qadariyya, Murji'a, Jabriyya, and Rawafid, which had no precedent in the time of the Salaf.

He then gives an example of such innovation: the Mu'tazila restricted Allah's will to obedience only, claiming He did not will disbelief and sins. This is a misguidance. The true teaching known to all Muslims, even the illiterate, is that all things occur by Allah's will: whatever He wills happens, whatever He does not will does not happen. The sinful ignorantly excuse themselves by Allah's will, but if Allah had willed good for them, they would not have sinned. Another example is the Mu'tazila's denial of the permissibility of pardon for those who die persisting in sins, denial of intercession, and denial of the creation of Paradise and Hell.

He then provides a decisive proof for his interpretation: the practice of Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz, who gave similar advice to someone asking about sectarian whims. He said to stick to the religion as the Salaf were upon and as the successors received from them, and to leave innovations.

Finally, the author turns the argument around: the very texts that those who favor taqlid use as warnings against nazar (inquiry) are actually evidence against taqlid. The Sunni scholars wrote books on tawhid to clarify the Salaf's belief, which was so well-known before innovations that even the simplest people adhered to it. They fortified it with rational proofs (barahin 'aqliyya) that lead to intellectual necessities, so that denying them excludes one from the community of rational people, and with definitive transmitted proofs (adilla naqliyya qat'iyya) where applicable. They built walls around the fortress of Islam when the countless armies of innovators attacked with doubts and illusions. Thus, the Salaf's statement, properly understood, supports the use of kalam to defend the faith.

Key Terms
سلف
تقليد
ابتداع
عجائز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Salaf's statement 'Stick to the religion of old women' prohibits all theological inquiry and supports blind following without reason.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the statement actually commands adherence to the Salaf's agreed creed and warns against innovations, neither of which prohibits using reason to understand and defend the faith.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the true meaning of the Salaf's statement 'Stick to the religion of old women'?
A) To blindly follow whatever the old women believe.
B) To adhere to the consensus of the righteous Salaf and abandon innovations.
C) To reject all forms of rational thinking in religion.
D) To only learn from old women and children.

Key Takeaway
The Salaf's saying 'Stick to the religion of old women' is not a rejection of knowledge but a directive to preserve the authentic creed and reject innovations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the phrase 'religion of old women', do you feel a subtle superiority in your own theological training, or do you recognize that the same essential creed is meant for both the scholar and the simple believer? How does this lesson humble your approach to learning and teaching Islam?
?
Have you ever dismissed the theological positions of the Salaf as simplistic, while unknowingly adopting an innovation that contradicts their unanimous agreement? Take a moment to identify one belief or practice you hold that might be rooted in a later sectarian development rather than the original creed. What will you do about it?
?
The knowledge of the Salaf's creed reached even old women and children without formal study. Does your own faith come from that same authentic transmission, or have you relied on modern interpretations that deviate from it? What concrete step will you take today to realign your understanding with what the righteous Salaf unanimously agreed upon?

Examples of Innovations: The Mu'tazila on Allah's Will and Other Matters
Sanusi provides examples of Mu'tazila innovations to clarify the Salaf's warning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will learn concrete examples of innovations introduced by the Mu'tazila that contradict the creed of the Salaf, especially regarding Allah's will, pardon, intercession, and creation of Paradise and Hell.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila innovated beliefs about Allah's will and the afterlife that were unknown to the Salaf.

To illustrate the innovations that the Salaf's statement warns against, Sanusi gives examples from the Mu'tazila. They restricted Allah's will to obedience only, denied that He wills disbelief and sins, and also denied the possibility of pardon for major sinners, intercession, and the creation of Paradise and Hell. These were not part of the Salaf's creed. Let's examine these examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the examples of Mu'tazila innovations
Sanusi lists three specific innovations: restricting Allah's will to obedience, denying pardon for persistent sinners, and denying intercession and creation of Paradise and Hell.
The first example is explained in detail.
2
Contrast with the true Aqeedah
The passage then states the true belief known to all: that all things occur by Allah's will, and what He wills happens.
This was so widely known that even the illiterate uttered it.
3
Understand the purpose
These examples show that the Salaf's command to stick to 'their religion' means holding onto these core beliefs and rejecting the innovations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني - وهو ما حكاه عن بعض السلف مِنْ قوله : ( عليكم بدين العجائز ) - : فلا دليلَ فيهِ أيضاً على صحة التقليد ؛ لأنَّ مراد هذا القائل : الأمرُ بالتمسُّكِ بما اجتمعَ عليه السلف الصالحُ مِنَ الصحابة والتابعين حتى وصل علمُهُ إلى مَنْ ليسَ أهلاً للنظرِ ؛ كالعجائز ، والصبيان في الكتاب ، والأعراب أهل البذو ، وترك ما أحدثه المبتدعةُ مِنَ القدريَّةِ والمرجئة والجبرية والروافض وغيرِهم ممَّا لا وجود له في أعصارِ السلف الصالح خاصهم وعامهم ، وذكرُ أمثلة ذلك على الاستيفاء يطولُ ، ولنذكرِ البعض ليتبيَّنَ بهِ المراد : فمِنْ ذلكَ : ما أحدثه المعتزلة مِنْ تقييد إرادة الله جل وعلا بالطاعة ، وأنَّ الكفر والمعاصي لم يُرِدْها اللهُ عزَّ وجلَّ ، وإِنَّما العباد أوقعوا ما لم يُرِدْهُ اللهُ جلَّ وعزَّ ، ومعلوم أنَّ هذهِ ضلالةٌ لا مستند لها ، وإِنَّما الذي اشتهر في زمن السلف الصالح وتلقاه منهم الخلف ، ولَهِجَ بهِ الصغير والكبير ، والذكر والأنثى ، والحـرُّ والعبد ، والحاضر والبادي ، حتى صار كَأَنَّهُ معلومٌ مِنْ دينِ المسلمينَ ضرورةً ، يَلهَجُ بهِ مَنْ عرف معناهُ ومَنْ لم يعرف .. وقوع الكائنات كلها بإرادةِ اللهِ تعالى ، وأنَّ ما شاءَ اللهُ كانَ ، وما لم يشأ لم يكن ، إنَّ جهلة العصاة يعتذرونَ عن معاصيهم بإرادة الله تعالى منهم حتى ذلكَ ، ولو أرادَ اللهُ تعالى بهم خيراً لما عَصَوا . ونحو هذا : ما أنكرَهُ المعتزلة مِنْ جوازِ العَفْوِ عَمَّنْ ماتَ مُصِرّاً على المعاصي ، وإنكار الشفاعة له ، وإنكار خلْقِ الجنَّةِ والنار ، ومثل هذا كثير في العقائدِ ويدلُّ قطعاً على هذا التأويل الذي ذكرناه : إتيان عمر بنِ عبدِ العزيز بمثل هذا جواباً للسائل عن الأهواء ، فكأنَّهُ قالَ لَهُ : عليكَ الدين بما كانَ عليه السلفُ ، وتلقَّاهُ منهم الخلفُ ، ودع ما يناقضُ ذلك ممَّا أحدثه المبتدعة . بل نقولُ : هذهِ الألفاظ التي اغترَّ بها مَنْ مَالَ إِلى التقليدِ ، وحَذَرَ مِنَ النظرِ في التوحيدِ .. هي في الحقيقةِ حُجَّةٌ عليهِ لا له ؛ لأنَّ علماء السنَّةِ رضوانُ اللهِ عليهم إنَّما ألَّفوا في علم التوحيد ليبينوا للناس ما كانَ عليه السلف الصالح ، وصارَ لشهرته ووضوحه قبل ظهورِ البدع ديناً لعجائزهم وإمائِهم وأهْلِ بذوهم وصبيانِ كُتَّابِهم ، وزادوا بأنْ حصنوه بالبراهين العقليّةِ التي تنتهي إلى ضرورات العقل ، بحيثُ يخرجُ مَنْ أنكرها عن ديوان العقلاء ، وبالأدلَّةِ النقليَّةِ القطعية فيما تقبلُ فيهِ ، فهم رضي الله عنهم جعلوا على حرزِ دين الإسلام أسواراً لما قدمَتْ جيوشُ المبتدعة التي لا تُحصى كثرة تريد استلابَ ذلك الدين وإبداله بجهالات يهلِكُ مَن اتَّبَعَها . ثم لمَّا أَتَتِ المبتدعةُ بمعاولِ الشبهات لتهدم بها أسوار الأدلَّةِ ، وبسلالم الأوهام والتخيُّلاتِ لتتجاوز بها إلى حرز الدين.. بالغَتْ
As for the second - which is what he reported from some of the Salaf of his saying: 'Stick to the religion of old women' -: there is no evidence in it either for the correctness of taqlid (blind following); because the intention of this speaker is the command to adhere to what the righteous Salaf from the Companions and the Followers unanimously agreed upon, until its knowledge reached those who are not qualified for independent inquiry (nazar) — like old women, children in the elementary school, and the Bedouins, the people of the desert — and to abandon what the innovators (mubtadi'a) invented among the Qadariyya, the Murji'a, the Jabriyya, the Rawafid, and others, which had no existence in the eras of the righteous Salaf, both their elite and commoners. And the exhaustive mention of examples of that would be lengthy, so we will mention some so that the intended meaning becomes clear: Among that is what the Mu'tazila innovated of restricting the will of Allah, Glorified and Exalted, to obedience, and that disbelief and sins Allah, Mighty and Majestic, did not will them, but rather the servants brought about what Allah, Glorified and Exalted, did not will. And it is known that this is a misguidance with no basis. Rather, what was famous in the time of the righteous Salaf and was received from them by the successors (khalaf), and was uttered by young and old, male and female, free and slave, the present and the desert dweller — until it became as if it were known from the religion of the Muslims by necessity, uttered by those who understand its meaning and those who do not — is: the occurrence of all things by the will of Allah, Most High, and that what Allah wills comes to be, and what He does not will does not come to be. Indeed, the ignorant sinners excuse their sins by the will of Allah, Most High, from them for that, and if Allah, Most High, had willed good for them, they would not have sinned. And similar to this is what the Mu'tazila denied of the permissibility of pardon for one who dies persisting in sins, and the denial of intercession for him, and the denial of the creation of Paradise and Hell. And such matters are many in the creed. And what indicates decisively for this interpretation which we mentioned is the coming of 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz with something like this as an answer to the questioner about sectarian whims (ahwa'). As if he said to him: 'Stick to the religion according to what the Salaf were upon, and the successors received from them, and leave whatever contradicts that of what the innovators have invented.' Rather we say: These very expressions by which those who inclined to taqlid and warned against inquiry (nazar) in tawhid were deluded... are in reality a proof against him, not for him; because the scholars of the Ahl al-Sunna, may Allah be pleased with them, only authored works on the science of tawhid to explain to the people what the righteous Salaf were upon, and it became — due to its fame and clarity before the emergence of innovations — the religion of their old women, their female servants, their people of the desert, and the children of their elementary schools. And they further fortified it with rational proofs (barahin 'aqliyya) that terminate in intellectual necessities, such that one who denies them leaves the register of rational people, and with definitive transmitted proofs (adilla naqliyya qat'iyya) in matters where such are accepted. So they — may Allah be pleased with them — made around the fortress of the religion of Islam walls when the armies of the innovators — whose multitude cannot be counted — advanced, seeking to plunder that religion and replace it with ignorances by which those who follow them will perish. Then when the innovators came with pickaxes of doubts to demolish thereby the walls of proofs, and with ladders of illusions and fantasies to transcend them to the fortress of religion... they intensified.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the argument that the statement attributed to some Salaf, 'Stick to the religion of old women,' supports blind taqlid and prohibits kalam (theological inquiry). The author, following the method of Sanusi, argues that this statement is actually a proof for kalam, not against it.

The passage begins by addressing the second objection: the report that some Salaf said 'Stick to the religion of old women.' The author denies that this supports taqlid. He explains that the speaker intended to command adherence to what the righteous Salaf (Companions and Followers) agreed upon, which eventually reached those who are not capable of independent inquiry, such as old women, schoolchildren, and Bedouins. He then gives an example of such innovation: the Mu'tazila restricted Allah's will to obedience only, claiming He did not will disbelief and sins. This is a misguidance. The true teaching known to all Muslims, even the illiterate, is that all things occur by Allah's will: whatever He wills happens, whatever He does not will does not happen. The sinful ignorantly excuse themselves by Allah's will, but if Allah had willed good for them, they would not have sinned. He then provides a decisive proof for his interpretation: the practice of Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz, who gave similar advice to someone asking about sectarian whims. He said to stick to the religion as the Salaf were upon and as the successors received from them, and to leave innovations.

They fortified it with rational proofs (barahin 'aqliyya) that lead to intellectual necessities, so that denying them excludes one from the community of rational people, and with definitive transmitted proofs (adilla naqliyya qat'iyya) where applicable. They built walls around the fortress of Islam when the countless armies of innovators attacked with doubts and illusions. Thus, the Salaf's statement, properly understood, supports the use of kalam to defend the faith.

Checkpoint
According to the Salaf, what is the correct belief about Allah's will regarding the occurrence of all things?
A) Allah wills only obedience and does not will disbelief or sins.
B) All things occur by Allah's will; what He wills happens, and what He does not will does not happen.
C) Humans create their own actions independently of Allah's will.
D) Allah's will is irrelevant to human actions.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila innovations, such as restricting Allah's will and denying pardon and intercession, contradict the Salaf's creed and illustrate what the Salaf warned against.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever find yourself attributing it to Allah's will to avoid taking responsibility? The lesson says that ignorant sinners use that excuse, but if Allah had willed good for you, you would not have sinned. How does this challenge your own excuses, and what will you do the next time you are tempted to blame Allah for your disobedience?
?
The Mu'tazila denied pardon and intercession for those who die persisting in sins. Does your hope in Allah's mercy ever lead you to be complacent about persisting in a sin you know you should stop? How can you hold onto both hope in Allah's mercy and the fear of dying without repentance, as the Salaf did?
?
Sanusi says the belief that all things occur by Allah's will was known by necessity among the Salaf, spoken by young and old. Do you truly live with that certainty? If you believed it fully, how would your reactions to both blessings and trials change starting today?

The Salaf's Statement as a Proof for Kalam
Sanusi turns the Salaf's statement into evidence supporting kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the very statement that some use to argue against kalam actually supports it, as the scholars of Ahl al-Sunna used rational proofs to defend the Salaf's creed.

Core Idea
The statement 'Stick to the religion of old women' actually proves the need for kalam to defend the faith.

After providing examples, Sanusi makes a powerful reversal: the Salaf's saying that some take as a prohibition against theological reasoning is actually a proof in favor of kalam. He points to the practice of Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz, who gave a similar answer, and then explains that Sunni scholars wrote works on tawhid to clarify the Salaf's creed and fortified it with rational proofs. This lesson explores that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the evidence from Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz
Sanusi cites that Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz gave a similar answer to someone asking about sectarian whims, commanding him to stick to the Salaf's religion and leave innovations.
This confirms Sanusi's interpretation.
2
Understand the reversal
Sanusi argues that the very expressions that taqlid supporters use are actually a proof against them, because Sunni scholars authored tawhid books to explain the Salaf's creed.
This shows the statement encourages knowing the Salaf's creed, not ignoring it.
3
Learn about the fortification of the creed
The scholars fortified the creed with rational proofs (barahin 'aqliyya) that lead to intellectual necessities, and with definitive transmitted proofs.
This is the essence of kalam: using reason to defend revelation.

استلابَ ذلك الدين وإبداله بجهالات يهلِكُ مَن اتَّبَعَها . ثم لمَّا أَتَتِ المبتدعةُ بمعاولِ الشبهات لتهدم بها أسوار الأدلَّةِ ، وبسلالم الأوهام والتخيُّلاتِ لتتجاوز بها إلى حرز الدين.. بالغَتْ
As for the second - which is what he reported from some of the Salaf of his saying: 'Stick to the religion of old women' -: there is no evidence in it either for the correctness of taqlid (blind following); because the intention of this speaker is the command to adhere to what the righteous Salaf from the Companions and the Followers unanimously agreed upon, until its knowledge reached those who are not qualified for independent inquiry (nazar) — like old women, children in the elementary school, and the Bedouins, the people of the desert — and to abandon what the innovators (mubtadi'a) invented among the Qadariyya, the Murji'a, the Jabriyya, the Rawafid, and others, which had no existence in the eras of the righteous Salaf, both their elite and commoners. And the exhaustive mention of examples of that would be lengthy, so we will mention some so that the intended meaning becomes clear: Among that is what the Mu'tazila innovated of restricting the will of Allah, Glorified and Exalted, to obedience, and that disbelief and sins Allah, Mighty and Majestic, did not will them, but rather the servants brought about what Allah, Glorified and Exalted, did not will. And it is known that this is a misguidance with no basis. Rather, what was famous in the time of the righteous Salaf and was received from them by the successors (khalaf), and was uttered by young and old, male and female, free and slave, the present and the desert dweller — until it became as if it were known from the religion of the Muslims by necessity, uttered by those who understand its meaning and those who do not — is: the occurrence of all things by the will of Allah, Most High, and that what Allah wills comes to be, and what He does not will does not come to be. Indeed, the ignorant sinners excuse their sins by the will of Allah, Most High, from them for that, and if Allah, Most High, had willed good for them, they would not have sinned. And similar to this is what the Mu'tazila denied of the permissibility of pardon for one who dies persisting in sins, and the denial of intercession for him, and the denial of the creation of Paradise and Hell. And such matters are many in the creed. And what indicates decisively for this interpretation which we mentioned is the coming of 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz with something like this as an answer to the questioner about sectarian whims (ahwa'). As if he said to him: 'Stick to the religion according to what the Salaf were upon, and the successors received from them, and leave whatever contradicts that of what the innovators have invented.' Rather we say: These very expressions by which those who inclined to taqlid and warned against inquiry (nazar) in tawhid were deluded... are in reality a proof against him, not for him; because the scholars of the Ahl al-Sunna, may Allah be pleased with them, only authored works on the science of tawhid to explain to the people what the righteous Salaf were upon, and it became — due to its fame and clarity before the emergence of innovations — the religion of their old women, their female servants, their people of the desert, and the children of their elementary schools. And they further fortified it with rational proofs (barahin 'aqliyya) that terminate in intellectual necessities, such that one who denies them leaves the register of rational people, and with definitive transmitted proofs (adilla naqliyya qat'iyya) in matters where such are accepted. So they — may Allah be pleased with them — made around the fortress of the religion of Islam walls when the armies of the innovators — whose multitude cannot be counted — advanced, seeking to plunder that religion and replace it with ignorances by which those who follow them will perish. Then when the innovators came with pickaxes of doubts to demolish thereby the walls of proofs, and with ladders of illusions and fantasies to transcend them to the fortress of religion... they intensified.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the argument that the statement attributed to some Salaf, 'Stick to the religion of old women,' supports blind taqlid and prohibits kalam (theological inquiry). The author, following the method of Sanusi, argues that this statement is actually a proof for kalam, not against it.

The passage begins by addressing the second objection: the report that some Salaf said 'Stick to the religion of old women.' The author denies that this supports taqlid. He explains that the speaker intended to command adherence to what the righteous Salaf (Companions and Followers) agreed upon, which eventually reached those who are not capable of independent inquiry, such as old women, schoolchildren, and Bedouins. The point is to stick to the consensus of the Salaf and to abandon the innovations introduced by groups like the Qadariyya, Murji'a, Jabriyya, and Rawafid, which had no precedent in the time of the Salaf.

He then gives an example of such innovation: the Mu'tazila restricted Allah's will to obedience only, claiming He did not will disbelief and sins. This is a misguidance. The true teaching known to all Muslims, even the illiterate, is that all things occur by Allah's will: whatever He wills happens, whatever He does not will does not happen. The sinful ignorantly excuse themselves by Allah's will, but if Allah had willed good for them, they would not have sinned. Another example is the Mu'tazila's denial of the permissibility of pardon for those who die persisting in sins, denial of intercession, and denial of the creation of Paradise and Hell.

He then provides a decisive proof for his interpretation: the practice of Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz, who gave similar advice to someone asking about sectarian whims. He said to stick to the religion as the Salaf were upon and as the successors received from them, and to leave innovations.

Finally, the author turns the argument around: the very texts that those who favor taqlid use as warnings against nazar (inquiry) are actually evidence against taqlid. The Sunni scholars wrote books on tawhid to clarify the Salaf's belief, which was so well-known before innovations that even the simplest people adhered to it. They fortified it with rational proofs (barahin 'aqliyya) that lead to intellectual necessities, so that denying them excludes one from the community of rational people, and with definitive transmitted proofs (adilla naqliyya qat'iyya) where applicable. They built walls around the fortress of Islam when the countless armies of innovators attacked with doubts and illusions. Thus, the Salaf's statement, properly understood, supports the use of kalam to defend the faith.

Key Terms
عمر بن عبد العزيز
براهين عقلية
أدلة نقلية
تقليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Salaf's statement 'Stick to the religion of old women' prohibits using rational arguments to defend the faith.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the statement is actually a proof for kalam because it motivated scholars to explain and defend the Salaf's creed using rational and transmitted proofs.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what do the actions of Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz and the Sunni scholars indicate about the true meaning of the Salaf's statement?
A) That it prohibits writing books on theology.
B) That it supports using rational proofs to defend the Salaf's creed.
C) That it only applies to old women.
D) That it encourages following one's own opinion.

Key Takeaway
The Salaf's saying, when properly understood, actually supports the use of kalam to defend the true creed from innovations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a doubt about your faith, do you reach for rational arguments or do you dismiss the question as unnecessary? What is one belief you hold that you could not defend with a clear proof if asked today?
?
The scholars built walls of proofs around the fortress of Islam. Are you inside those walls, or have you left them unguarded, assuming the fortress will stand without effort? What will you do this week to reinforce one area of your own intellectual defense?
?
The 'religion of old women' was praised for its clarity before innovations appeared. In your own life, where have you confused simplicity with ignorance, and where have you neglected to arm yourself with knowledge for fear of complexity? How will you seek both clarity and depth starting today?

The Danger of Abandoning Rational Theology
Sanusi cites Mu'tazilite errors and the Salaf's response to justify kalām.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi begins his defense of kalām by citing examples of heretical beliefs and the response of the early caliph Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz.

Core Idea
Blind rejection of theology leaves the religion vulnerable to distortion.

Sanusi opens his defense of speculative theology by showing the real consequences of abandoning reasoned theology. He points to the Mu'tazilites, who denied core doctrines like intercession and the creation of Paradise and Hell, and then recalls how the respected caliph Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz instructed a questioner to hold fast to the way of the Salaf and reject innovations. This sets the stage for his central argument: that the scholars of the Sunnah did not reject rational discourse but used it to protect the faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the examples of Mu'tazilite denials
Notice the specific doctrines the Mu'tazila rejected: pardon for grave sinners, intercession, and creation of Paradise and Hell. Ask yourself: What would happen to the creed if these were left unchallenged?
These examples show the danger of purely fideistic approaches.
2
Focus on the response of Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz
The caliph tells the questioner to adhere to the religion of the Salaf and reject innovations. Understand that this is used as evidence that the early authorities condemned innovation, not rational theology itself.
This foreshadows the author's main point.
3
Reflect on the connection to kalām
Ask yourself: How does this passage begin to answer those who forbid kalām? The author is showing that the Salaf themselves were not silent in the face of error.
